5 min
The Requirement of Specifying Type and Quantity in Claims
Establishes the requirement of specifying type and quantity with an exception for mortgage and usurpation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of specifying type and quantity for a valid claim and identify the exceptions for mortgage and usurpation.

Core Idea
A lawsuit is invalid without specifying type and quantity, except for mortgage and usurpation.

In Hanafi judiciary, the validity of a lawsuit depends on the plaintiff specifying the type (جنس) and quantity (قدر) of the claimed item. This ensures certainty and prevents ambiguity. However, when dealing with mortgage and usurpation, the jurists made an exception based on the nature of these transactions. This lesson explores the general rule and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Find the statement that the claim is not valid until the type and quantity are known. Note the phrase: 'ولا تصح الدعوى حتى يذكر شيئاً علم جنسه وقدره'.
This is the foundational principle.
2
Locate the Exception
The text explicitly excepts claims for mortgage and usurpation. Underline the phrase 'يستثنى من فساد الدعوى بالمجهول دعوى الرهن والغصب'.
3
Analyze the Proof
The proof is a narration from Rahn al-Asl: witnesses testify about a mortgaged garment without specifying which garment, yet their testimony is accepted. This shows the exception.
The proof is based on practice and necessity.
4
Understand the Rationale
In mortgage and usurpation, the exact item is often unknown to witnesses, but the claim is still valid because the nature of the transaction requires flexibility.
The exception is limited to these two cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح الدعوى حتى يذكر شيئاً علم جنسه وقدره وإن كان عيناً في يد المدعى عليه ويستثنى من فساد الدعوى بالمجهول دعوى الرهن والغصب لما في الخانية معزياً إلى رهن الأصل : إذا شهدوا أنه رهن عنده ثوباً ولم يسموا الثوب ولم يعرفوا عينه جازت شهادتهم والقول للمرتهن في أي ثوب كان وكذلك في الغصب ا هـ. فالدعوى بالأولى ولم أر اشتراط لفظ مخصوص للدعوى وينبغي اشتراط ما يدل على الجزم والتحقيق، ولو قال أشك أو أظن لم تصح الدعوى. ولم يشترط المصنف بيان السبب وفيه تفصيل ؛ فإن كان المدعي ديناً لم يشترط وللقاضي أن يسأله عن سببه فإن لم يبين لم يجبر كما في الخانية ولو كان المدعي مكيلاً فلا بد من ذكر سبب الوجوب لاختلاف الأحكام باختلاف الأسباب حتى إن من سلم يحتاج إلى مكان الإيفاء ويمنع الاستبدال قبل قبضه وثمنه المبيع بخلافه فيهما وإن من قرض لا يلزم التأجيل ا هـ . ثم قال : وفي دعوى اللحم لا بد من بيان السبب وكذا في دعوى الكعك ا هـ .
And the claim is not valid until he mentions something whose type and quantity are known, even if it is a physical object in the hand of the defendant. And an exception is made from the invalidity of a claim due to unknownness for claims of mortgage and usurpation, as in Al-Khaniyyah quoting from Rahn al-Asl: If they testify that he mortgaged a garment with him but they did not name the garment and did not know its specific identity, their testimony is permissible, and the statement of the mortgagee regarding which garment it was is accepted. Similarly for usurpation. End. So the claim is even more [valid for claim]. And I have not seen a specific wording required for the claim, but it is advisable to require wording indicating certainty and verification. If he says 'I doubt' or 'I think,' the claim is not valid. And the author did not require stating the cause. But there is detail: If the claim is for a debt, stating the cause is not required, but the judge may ask for it; if he does not explain, he is not forced, as in Al-Khaniyyah. But if the claim is for a measured commodity (makīl), then it is necessary to mention the cause of obligation because rulings differ based on causes. For example, from a sale requires a place of delivery and prevents substitution before possession, and the price differs, while from a loan does not entail a delay. End. Then he said: In a claim for meat, it is necessary to state the cause, and similarly for a claim for ka'k (a type of bread). End.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage establishes a fundamental rule: a lawsuit (دعوى) is not valid unless the plaintiff specifies the type (جنس) and quantity (قدر) of the thing claimed. This applies even if the object is in the defendant's possession. However, there is an exception for claims concerning mortgage (رهن) and usurpation (غصب), based on a transmission from Al-Khaniyyah quoting Rahn al-Asl. In those cases, if the witness testifies that the defendant mortgaged a garment but does not specify which garment, the testimony is accepted, and the mortgagee's statement about which garment it was is taken as true. The same applies to usurpation. The commentator notes that no specific wording is required for a lawsuit, but it should indicate certainty (جزم and تحقيق). If the plaintiff expresses doubt, the claim is invalid. The author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) did not require stating the cause (sabab) of the claim, but there is detail: for a debt, the cause is not required but the judge may ask; for measured items (like grains), the cause must be stated because different causes (sale, loan, etc.) have different legal consequences.

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): Lawsuit or legal claim
جنس (jins): Type or genus of the claimed object
قدر (qadr): Quantity or amount
رهن (rahn): Mortgage or pledge
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation or wrongful taking
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or testimony

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the general rule for the validity of a lawsuit according to this passage?
A) The plaintiff must be certain
B) The claim must specify type and quantity
C) The claim must be in writing
D) The defendant must be present

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a valid claim must specify type and quantity; mortgage and usurpation claims are exempt from this requirement.

intermediate
6 min
The Requirement of Stating the Cause in Claims
Details the requirement of stating the cause in claims, distinguishing between debts and measured commodities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when stating the cause of obligation is required in a claim and apply the rule to debts versus measured commodities.

Core Idea
Stating the cause of obligation is necessary for claims of measured commodities but not for debts, due to differing legal consequences.

In addition to specifying type and quantity, Hanafi jurists also discuss whether the plaintiff must state the cause (sabab) of the claimed right. The rule differs depending on whether the claim is for a debt or for a measured commodity (makil). This lesson explains the details and the rationale behind the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Position
The author did not require stating the cause, but the commentator introduces a detail. Note the phrase 'ولم يشترط المصنف بيان السبب وفيه تفصيل'.
2
Distinguish Debts from Measured Commodities
For a debt (دين), stating the cause is not required; the judge may ask but cannot force. For a measured commodity (مكيل), stating the cause is necessary.
The cause could be sale (بيع), loan (قرض), etc.
3
Apply to Specific Examples
The passage gives examples: claims for meat and ka'k (a type of bread) require stating the cause. Understand why these are treated like measured commodities.
4
Understand the Rationale
Different causes lead to different legal consequences (e.g., sale requires place of delivery, loan does not entail delay). Without knowing the cause, the judge cannot rule correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح الدعوى حتى يذكر شيئاً علم جنسه وقدره وإن كان عيناً في يد المدعى عليه ويستثنى من فساد الدعوى بالمجهول دعوى الرهن والغصب لما في الخانية معزياً إلى رهن الأصل : إذا شهدوا أنه رهن عنده ثوباً ولم يسموا الثوب ولم يعرفوا عينه جازت شهادتهم والقول للمرتهن في أي ثوب كان وكذلك في الغصب ا هـ. فالدعوى بالأولى ولم أر اشتراط لفظ مخصوص للدعوى وينبغي اشتراط ما يدل على الجزم والتحقيق، ولو قال أشك أو أظن لم تصح الدعوى. ولم يشترط المصنف بيان السبب وفيه تفصيل ؛ فإن كان المدعي ديناً لم يشترط وللقاضي أن يسأله عن سببه فإن لم يبين لم يجبر كما في الخانية ولو كان المدعي مكيلاً فلا بد من ذكر سبب الوجوب لاختلاف الأحكام باختلاف الأسباب حتى إن من سلم يحتاج إلى مكان الإيفاء ويمنع الاستبدال قبل قبضه وثمنه المبيع بخلافه فيهما وإن من قرض لا يلزم التأجيل ا هـ . ثم قال : وفي دعوى اللحم لا بد من بيان السبب وكذا في دعوى الكعك ا هـ .
And the claim is not valid until he mentions something whose type and quantity are known, even if it is a physical object in the hand of the defendant. And an exception is made from the invalidity of a claim due to unknownness for claims of mortgage and usurpation, as in Al-Khaniyyah quoting from Rahn al-Asl: If they testify that he mortgaged a garment with him but they did not name the garment and did not know its specific identity, their testimony is permissible, and the statement of the mortgagee regarding which garment it was is accepted. And the author did not require stating the cause. But there is detail: If the claim is for a debt, stating the cause is not required, but the judge may ask for it; if he does not explain, he is not forced, as in Al-Khaniyyah. But if the claim is for a measured commodity (makīl), then it is necessary to mention the cause of obligation because rulings differ based on causes. For example, from a sale requires a place of delivery and prevents substitution before possession, and the price differs, while from a loan does not entail a delay. End. Then he said: In a claim for meat, it is necessary to state the cause, and similarly for a claim for ka'k (a type of bread). End.

This applies even if the object is in the defendant's possession. Key Terms
سبب (sabab): Cause or reason for the obligation (e.g., sale, loan)
مكيل (makil): Measured commodity (by volume, e.g., grain)
دين (dayn): Debt
لحم (lahm): Meat
كعك (ka'k): A type of bread or cake

Key Terms from Source Passage

For which of the following claims is it necessary to state the cause (sabab) according to the passage?

C) A claim for a car
D) A claim for a house

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, stating the cause of the claim is necessary for measured commodities and similar items, but not for debts.

Specifying Type and Quantity for Various Items in Claims
Specification requirements per item for valid claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific description requirements for weighed items, quince, bread, silk, cotton, henna, and brocade in Hanafi claims.

Core Idea
Each item has unique required descriptors—knowing them prevents invalid claims.

In Hanafi law, a claim (da'wa) must specify the type and quantity of the object. However, the required specifications vary by item. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the precise descriptions needed for common items such as quince, bread, silk, cotton, henna, and brocade. Understanding these requirements is essential for drafting valid claims and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item category
Determine if the item is weighed, fruit, bread, fabric, etc. Each category has distinct specification rules.
2
Check the weight requirement
For weighed items (mawzoonat), weight must be stated. For quince, weight is necessary due to size variation.
Claims for mortar (?) also require weight.
3
Note additional descriptors
List extra attributes for each item: taste (sweet/sour) for quince; color (white/black) and sesame amount for bread; origin (Bukhari/Khwarizmi) for cotton; processing (ground/leaves) for henna.
4
Distinguish salam from 'ayn
For brocade in a salam contract, full descriptions and weight are needed; for a specific object ('ayn), weight is not required and only basic descriptions suffice.
The text mentions 'mention the needles' (الأبر) which is unclear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المؤلف رحمه الله باشتراط معلومية الجنس والقدر إلى أنه لا بد من بيان الوزن في الموزونان، وفي دعوى وقررمان أو سفرجل لا بد من ذكر الوزن للتفاوت في الوقر، ويذكر أنه حلو أو حامض أو صغير أو من دقيق المغسول أو من غيره وما عليه من السمسم أنه كبير، وفي دعوى الكعك يذكر أنه أبيض أو أسود وقدر السمسم، وقيل لا حاجة إلى السمسم وقدره وصفته. وفي دعوى لأبريسم بسبب السلم لا حاجة إلى ذكر الشرائط، والمختار أنه لا بد من ذكر الشرائط، وفي القطن يشترط بيان أنه بخاري أو خوارزمي، وفي الحناء لا بد من بيان أنه مدقوق أو ورق، وفي الديباج إن سلماً يذكر الأوصاف والوزن، وإن عيناً لا حاجة إلى ذكر الوزن ويذكر الأبر قوله : (في هبة العبد) أي في الهبة للعبد قوله : (ويستثنى من فساد الدعوى بالمجهول دعوى الرهن والغصب) أقول وفي المعراج وفساد الدعوى إما أن لا يكون لزمه شيء على الخصم أو حقاً يكون المدعي مجهولاً في نفسه ولا يعلم فيه خلاف إلا في الوصية بأن ادعى . من وصية أو إقرار فإنهما يصحان بالمجهول وتصح دعوى الإبراء المجهول بلا خلاف .اهـ. فبلغت المستثنيات خمسة تأمل
And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated by requiring knowledge of type and quantity that it is necessary to state the weight for weighed items. And in a claim for mortar (?) or quince, it is necessary to mention the weight because of the difference in heaviness. And it should be mentioned whether it is sweet or sour, small or made from washed flour or other, and what sesame is on it, that it is large. And in a claim for ka'k, it should be mentioned whether it is white or black and the amount of sesame. And it is said there is no need for sesame and its amount and description. And in a claim for silk due to salam, there is no need to mention conditions, but the chosen view is that it is necessary to mention conditions. And in cotton, it is required to state whether it is Bukhārī or Khwārizmī. And in henna, it is necessary to state whether it is ground or leaves. And in brocade, if it is a salam, the descriptions and weight are mentioned; if it is a specific object, there is no need to mention weight, and mention the needles? His saying: (in the gift of the slave) meaning the gift to the slave. His saying: (and an exception is made from the invalidity of a claim due to unknownness for claims of mortgage and usurpation.) I say: In Al-Mi'raj, the invalidity of a claim is either because nothing is owed to the opponent or because the plaintiff is unknown in himself. And I do not know of a disagreement except in the case of a bequest or acknowledgment, for they are valid with unknownness. And a claim of unknown acquittal is valid without disagreement. End. So the exceptions reach five. Reflect.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the requirement of specifying type and quantity for various items. For weighed items (موزونات), the weight must be stated. For mortar or quince, weight is necessary because their weight varies. For bread (كعك), color and amount of sesame should be stated, though some say sesame is not needed. For silk in a salam contract, some say conditions are not needed, but the chosen view (المختار) is that they are. For cotton, the origin (Bukhari or Khwarizmi) must be specified. For henna, whether ground or leaves. For brocade, in salam, weight and descriptions are required; if it is a specific item, weight is not needed. Then the commentator adds that the author also mentioned exceptions to the rule of invalidity due to unknownness: in addition to mortgage and usurpation, there are also bequest (وصية), acknowledgment (إقرار), and acquittal (إبراء). This makes five exceptions.

Key Terms
موزون (mawzoon): Weighed item (sold by weight)
سفرجل (safarjal): Quince (a fruit)
كعك (ka'k): A type of bread or biscuit
إبريسم (ibrisim): Silk
سلم (salam): Advance sale (pay now, deliver later)
قطن (qutn): Cotton
حناء (hina'): Henna (a plant used for dye)
ديباج (dibaj): Brocade (a rich fabric)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which item requires stating its origin (e.g., Bukhari or Khwarizmi) in a claim?
A) Quince
B) Cotton
C) Silk
D) Henna

Key Takeaway
Each item has unique specification requirements; general rule of type and quantity is not enough for validity.

Exceptions to the Requirement of Specification: Bequest, Acknowledgment, Acquittal
Exceptions to specification requirement for validity of claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five exceptions to the rule that claims must specify type and quantity, and explain why bequest, acknowledgment, and acquittal are accepted despite unknownness.

Core Idea
Bequest, acknowledgment, and acquittal bypass the specification requirement because they involve rights that can be established without precise quantification.

While Hanafi law generally requires claims to specify type and quantity, certain legal transactions are accepted even if the subject is unknown. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists mortgage, usurpation, bequest, acknowledgment, and acquittal as exceptions. Understanding these exceptions is vital for lawyers and judges dealing with cases where the exact details are unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Generally, a claim (da'wa) must specify type and quantity to avoid invalidity due to jahalah (unknownness).
2
List the exceptions
The text mentions five exceptions: mortgage (رهن), usurpation (غصب), bequest (وصية), acknowledgment (إقرار), and acquittal (إبراء).
Mortgage and usurpation were already known; the commentator adds three more.
3
Understand the rationale
For bequest, the testator may not recall details; for acknowledgment, the debtor admits a right; for acquittal, it is a release of debt. In all three, the party acknowledging the right does so freely, and imposing strict specification would defeat the purpose.
Acquittal (ibra') is accepted without disagreement (bila khilaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المؤلف رحمه الله باشتراط معلومية الجنس والقدر إلى أنه لا بد من بيان الوزن في الموزونان، وفي دعوى وقررمان أو سفرجل لا بد من ذكر الوزن للتفاوت في الوقر، ويذكر أنه حلو أو حامض أو صغير أو من دقيق المغسول أو من غيره وما عليه من السمسم أنه كبير، وفي دعوى الكعك يذكر أنه أبيض أو أسود وقدر السمسم، وقيل لا حاجة إلى السمسم وقدره وصفته. وفي دعوى لأبريسم بسبب السلم لا حاجة إلى ذكر الشرائط، والمختار أنه لا بد من ذكر الشرائط، وفي القطن يشترط بيان أنه بخاري أو خوارزمي، وفي الحناء لا بد من بيان أنه مدقوق أو ورق، وفي الديباج إن سلماً يذكر الأوصاف والوزن، وإن عيناً لا حاجة إلى ذكر الوزن ويذكر الأبر قوله : (في هبة العبد) أي في الهبة للعبد قوله : (ويستثنى من فساد الدعوى بالمجهول دعوى الرهن والغصب) أقول وفي المعراج وفساد الدعوى إما أن لا يكون لزمه شيء على الخصم أو حقاً يكون المدعي مجهولاً في نفسه ولا يعلم فيه خلاف إلا في الوصية بأن ادعى . من وصية أو إقرار فإنهما يصحان بالمجهول وتصح دعوى الإبراء المجهول بلا خلاف .اهـ. فبلغت المستثنيات خمسة تأمل
And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated by requiring knowledge of type and quantity that it is necessary to state the weight for weighed items. And in a claim for mortar (?) or quince, it is necessary to mention the weight because of the difference in heaviness. And it should be mentioned whether it is sweet or sour, small or made from washed flour or other, and what sesame is on it, that it is large. And in a claim for ka'k, it should be mentioned whether it is white or black and the amount of sesame. And it is said there is no need for sesame and its amount and description. And in a claim for silk due to salam, there is no need to mention conditions, but the chosen view is that it is necessary to mention conditions. And in cotton, it is required to state whether it is Bukhārī or Khwārizmī. And in henna, it is necessary to state whether it is ground or leaves. And in brocade, if it is a salam, the descriptions and weight are mentioned; if it is a specific object, there is no need to mention weight, and mention the needles? His saying: (in the gift of the slave) meaning the gift to the slave. His saying: (and an exception is made from the invalidity of a claim due to unknownness for claims of mortgage and usurpation.) I say: In Al-Mi'raj, the invalidity of a claim is either because nothing is owed to the opponent or because the plaintiff is unknown in himself. And I do not know of a disagreement except in the case of a bequest or acknowledgment, for they are valid with unknownness. And a claim of unknown acquittal is valid without disagreement. End. So the exceptions reach five. Reflect.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the requirement of specifying type and quantity for various items. For weighed items (موزونات), the weight must be stated. For mortar or quince, weight is necessary because their weight varies. For bread (كعك), color and amount of sesame should be stated, though some say sesame is not needed. For silk in a salam contract, some say conditions are not needed, but the chosen view (المختار) is that they are. For cotton, the origin (Bukhari or Khwarizmi) must be specified. For henna, whether ground or leaves. For brocade, in salam, weight and descriptions are required; if it is a specific item, weight is not needed. This makes five exceptions.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyyah): Bequest or will
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment (confession of a right)
إبراء (ibra'): Acquittal or discharge (release from debt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many total exceptions are there to the rule that a claim must specify type and quantity?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 5
D) 4

Key Takeaway
Bequest, acknowledgment, and acquittal are valid even if the subject is unknown, making them exceptions to the general rule of specification.

Obligation to Produce Claimed Items in Court: Exceptions for Trusts and Heavy Items
Explains obligation to produce claimed item in court and exceptions for trusts and heavy items.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general obligation to bring a claimed item to court, identify the two exceptions (trusts and items with carriage/expense), and apply the definition of carriage and expense to determine whether a defendant is compelled to produce the item.

Core Idea
The defendant must produce the claimed item in court unless it is a trust or too heavy/costly to move.

In judicial proceedings, the defendant is generally required to bring the disputed item to court for inspection. However, Hanafi law recognizes practical exceptions: if the item is a trust (wadi‘a), the bailee only needs to vacate possession, not physically transport it. Similarly, items that require significant expense or effort to move are exempted. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq expands on these exceptions and provides multiple definitions for what constitutes 'carriage and expense'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State the default obligation of the defendant when a plaintiff claims a specific item in his possession.
The general rule is that the defendant must bring the item to court.
2
Recognize the exceptions
List the two exceptions mentioned: trusts and items with carriage/expense. For trusts, the obligation is to vacate, not to transport.
These exceptions relieve the defendant from physical production.
3
Analyze the definition of carriage and expense
Examine the three interpretations: (a) cannot be carried with one hand, (b) requires expense to transport, (c) price varies by location.
Any one of these criteria suffices to qualify as having carriage/expense.
4
Apply istishab to past possession
If the plaintiff proves past possession and the defendant does not prove exit, the court presumes continued possession and compels production.
This is based on the presumption of continuity (istishab).
5
Distinguish between bringing and vacating
Explain the difference: actual delivery vs. releasing control. For trusts, only vacating (takhliyah) is required, not physical transport.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the bailee's obligation.

Its value, and if he claims real estate, he mentions its boundaries, and three suffice, and the names of its owners, and it is necessary to mention the grandfather. [If it is] a trust with him, there is no order to bring it, because the obligation therein is vacating, not transporting. So if the possessor denies, the production would be correct, and he would be right. He claimed a specific item in his hand and wanted to bring it to the court, but the defendant denied it being in his hand, so the plaintiff proved that it was in the defendant's hand a year before this date. Is it accepted and is the defendant compelled to bring it by this evidence or not? This was an incident of fatwa, and it should be accepted if it is established in his hand in the past and his ownership [of it] is not established so it remains and does not disappear due to doubt. End. He made absolute the obligation to bring it, but it is restricted to that which has no carriage or expense. As for that which has carriage and expense, the defendant is not compelled to bring it. The interpretation of carriage and expense is a situation where it is carried to the court for a fee, not free. So this is what has carriage and expense. And it was mentioned two folios later that what cannot be carried with one hand is what has carriage and expense. And it was said: what needs expense to transport it, like wheat and barley, is what has carriage and expense, not what does not need expense to transport it, like musk and a little saffron. And it was said: what differs in price in different lands is what has carriage and expense, not what agrees. End. Then he mentioned therein issues regarding when the claimant describes the claim and when it is present it differs in some respects. The summary is that if he abandons the first claim and claims the present one, it is heard because it is a new claim, otherwise not. And by what we have established, it is known that in the speech of the author and others there is leniency, because in a claim of a specific trust item, he is not required to bring it; rather he is required to vacate it.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā’iq. It addresses the obligation of the defendant to bring the claimed item to court. The general rule is that the defendant must produce the item, but there are two key exceptions: (1) trusts (wadī‘a): the bailee is only required to vacate the item, not physically bring it to court, because the obligation is to release possession, not to transport; (2) items with heavy carriage and expense (ḥaml wa mu’na): if moving the item requires payment or significant effort, the defendant is not compelled to bring it. The passage clarifies the definition of 'carriage and expense' through multiple interpretations: (a) anything that cannot be carried by one hand, (b) anything whose transport requires expense (like grain), (c) anything whose price varies by location. Additionally, the passage discusses the scenario where the plaintiff proves past possession and the court presumes continued possession (istishāb). It notes that the author's wording is somewhat lenient, as for a trust, only vacating is required, not production.

Key Terms
التخلية (al-takhliyah): Vacating or releasing possession; the obligation of a bailee is to surrender the item, not to transport it.
الحمل والمؤنة (al-ḥaml wa al-mu’na): Carriage and expense; the cost and effort of moving an item to court.
الوديعة (al-wadī‘a): A trust or deposit; an item entrusted to someone for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the obligation of a bailee (trustee) regarding a claimed trust item?
A) He must bring the item to court personally.
B) He is only required to vacate the item, not transport it.
C) He must pay the value of the item.
D) He is not required to do anything until the plaintiff proves his claim.

Key Takeaway
The defendant's duty to bring the claimed item is not absolute; it is conditioned on the item being movable without significant expense, and trusts require only release of possession.

Specifying Value When the Claimed Item is Absent and Exceptions
Explains the rule and exceptions for stating value when item is absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule for specifying value when the claimed item is absent and identify the exceptions for usurpation (ghasb) and pledge (rahn).

Core Idea
Claim value is required when the item is gone, but not for usurpation or pledge—practical need overrides strictness.

In a Hanafi court, when a claimant cannot produce the physical item due to destruction or absence, they must state its value to make the claim known. However, this requirement is relaxed in cases of usurpation (ghasb) and pledge (rahn), where the focus is on the wrongful act or contract rather than the precise value. This lesson explores the rule, its rationale, and the exceptions that reflect the principle of istihsan (juristic preference).

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
From the passage, find the statement that if the claimed item is absent ('it is difficult to mention its value'), the claimant must specify the value. Note the reason: the description alone is not enough to identify the item, and viewing it is impossible.
The rule is stated at the very beginning: 'If it is difficult to mention its value... then it is necessary to mention its value.'
2
Understand the Exceptions
Look for the phrases 'except for' or 'they excluded from it claims of usurpation and pledge.' The passage explicitly states that in ghasb and rahn claims, value specification is not required. Identify the basis: the nature of these claims (wrongful taking or security) makes the precise value secondary.
The exceptions are justified by istihsan (juristic preference) and are supported by the majority of Hanafi scholars (ammat al-mashāyikh).
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Note that some Hanafi scholars (some mashāyikh) still require value in ghasb/rahn claims, but the mufta bihi (operative opinion) is the majority view that does not require it. Understand that this is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
The passage mentions 'some of our scholars said: his claim is only heard if he mentions the value,' but then states 'the majority of scholars hold that this claim and evidence are accepted.'
4
Analyze the Role of Istishab
The passage mentions istishab (presumption of continuity) in the context of the proof for the claim. According to Hanafi usul, istishab is a proof for defense (daf‘), not for establishing claims (ithbat). Consider why this distinction is relevant: the claimant is establishing a claim, so istishab cannot be used to presume the item's value or existence.
This usul principle reinforces the need for explicit value specification when the item is absent, except where istihsan overrides.
5
Apply to Practical Scenarios
Work through concrete scenarios: a theft claim (value needed to check nisab), a ghasb claim (value not needed), a claim for a present item (value not needed), and a claim for multiple items (total value may suffice). Use the passage's discussion of al-Khaniyya and Jami‘ al-Fusulayn.
The passage also addresses animals: if the animal is outside, the judge may go out or send someone; the value discussion there is about how to handle the item's presence.

His statement: (If it is difficult to mention its value) meaning due to its destruction or absence, then it is necessary to mention its value to make the claim known, because an item is not known by description, and value is known by it, and viewing the item has become difficult. We only qualified difficulty with destruction or absence to avoid the millstone, heap of food, and similar things whose existence is difficult to maintain, for the judge sends his agent as we have mentioned, and he is not satisfied with mentioning the value. And regarding an animal, the judge may choose: if he wishes, he goes out, and if he wishes, he sends someone to hear the claim and testimony in its presence, as in Jami‘ al-Fusulayn. And in it: if he claims different items of different categories, types, and descriptions, and mentions the total value collectively, and does not mention the value of each item individually, the scholars differed: some said it is necessary to detail, and some said summary suffices, and that is the correct one. End. And in al-Sirajiyya: if he claims slaves, he should mention their kind, age, description, physical features, and value, and if the claimant is present, pointing suffices. The apparent meaning of the author and others is that the condition of mentioning value is only when bringing the item is difficult; before the difficulty appears, it is not required. He said in al-Khaniyya: The value is only required in a claim if it is a theft claim to know if it is a nisab or not; otherwise, there is no need to mention it. End. And he made absolute the obligation to mention value when it is difficult, and they excluded from it claims of usurpation and pledge. In Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: if he claims an absent item, it is not possible for him to bring it, so he claims that a garment or a slave was usurped from him, and he does not know its existence or destruction, then if he mentions the kind, description, and value, his claim is accepted; if he does not mention its value, it is indicated in most books that it is accepted. For it is mentioned in the Book of Pledge: if he claims that a garment is pledged with him and he denies, his claim is heard. And it is mentioned in the Book of Usurpation: he claims that a slave-girl was usurped from him, and he brings proof, his claim is heard. And some of our scholars said: his claim is only heard if he mentions the value. This is an interpretation of what is mentioned in the book that the witnesses testified to the defendant's confession of usurpation, so usurpation of the slave is established by his confession for the purpose of detention and judgment. And the majority of scholars hold that this claim and evidence are accepted, but for detention. And the absolute statement of Muhammad in the book indicates it. The meaning of detention is that he detains him until he brings [the item] to present evidence on the specific item. So if he says, 'I am not able to [bring] it,' he is detained as much as if he were able to bring it, then judgment is passed against him for its value. End. The summary is that in claims of usurpation and pledge, it is not required to mention the kind and value for the validity of the claim and testimony, and the say regarding the value is that of the usurper and mortgagee. Then know that what he mentioned is called in usul al-fiqh istishab (presumption of continuity), and it is a proof for defense, not for establishing claims. There is no doubt that what is mentioned is from the category of establishing claims. The author of al-Tawdih said: Among the invalid proofs is istishab; it is a proof according to al-Shafi‘i in everything whose existence is established by evidence and then doubt occurs about its continuation, but according to us, it is proof for defense, not for establishing claims, because the evidence that establishes does not indicate continuation. This is apparent from his statement: 'And regarding the animal, the judge may choose etc.' And he said in Ghayat al-Bayan: If it is an animal and the judge's eye cannot see it, and it is not possible for the witnesses and claimant to point, and it is at the door of the mosque, he commands that it be brought in, for that is permissible when there is need. Do you not see that the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed tawaf around the House on his she-camel, even though the sanctity of the Sacred Mosque is greater than that of other mosques? And if the judge's eye can see it, he does not bring it in because he cannot be safe from what it might do, and the need is absent. End. His statement: (And in al-Sirajiyya: he claims slaves etc.) The apparent meaning is that it is based on the first opinion as opposed to the correct one. Consider. His statement: (He said in al-Khaniyya: The value is only required etc.) Some virtuous people transmitted from Shaykh ‘Umar, the author of al-Nahr, brother of the author: 'The meaning should be that if the item is present, it is not required to mention its value except in a theft claim.' End. I say: So it would have been better for the author to say before the phrase of al-Khaniyya: 'But if it is present, then no,' instead of his statement 'But before the appearance of difficulty, then no.' His statement: 'Then judgment is passed against him for its value' – he did not explain the ruling when he does not know its value.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn from Kanz al-Daqā’iq. It provides the rule: if the claimed item is destroyed or absent, the claimant must specify its value so the claim is known. The commentary clarifies that this is only when the item cannot be viewed; otherwise, the judge sends an agent or views it himself. For animals, the judge may go to the item or send a deputy. For multiple items of different types, the majority opinion (al-sahih) is that a total value suffices without detailing each. The Khaniyya states that value is only required in theft claims to check the nisab; otherwise not. For usurpation (ghasb) and pledge (rahn), the requirement is relaxed: the claim is valid even without specifying value or type because the claim is based on the act of usurpation or contract, and the value is later determined by the defendant's statement (qawl) unless proven otherwise. The passage also discusses istishab (presumption of continuity) as a proof for defense only, not for establishing a claim, citing al-Tawdih. Finally, it addresses the ruling on bringing an animal into the mosque for testimony if the judge cannot see it, using the Prophet's tawaf on his she-camel as analogy.

Key Terms
الاستصحاب (al-istishāb): Presumption of continuity: assuming a state continues unless proven otherwise.
الغصب (al-ghaṣb): Usurpation: taking someone's property unlawfully and forcibly.
الرهن (al-rahn): Pledge: a contract where property is given as security for a debt.
النصاب (al-niṣāb): Minimum threshold for which a theft claim is actionable (theft penalty requires nisab).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi court, what is the ruling on a claim of usurpation (ghasb) where the claimant does not specify the value of the usurped item?
A) The claim is invalid and cannot be heard.
B) The claim is valid, but the court will not proceed until value is stated.
C) The claim is valid and heard; value is later determined by the usurper's statement.
D) The claim is only valid if the item is present.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi courts, value must be stated when the claimed item is absent, but exceptions for usurpation and pledge remove this requirement, reflecting istihsan to facilitate justice.

Conditions for Claims of Value, Deposit, and Usurpation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on specification requirements for claims of value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specification requirements for a claim of value of a consumed item and explain the two Hanafi disputes on specifying gender and on mixed goods.

Core Idea
Claiming value of consumed goods requires genus and type; gender is disputed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq outlines the procedural conditions for filing a claim for the value of a consumed item. The Hanafi school agrees that genus and type must be specified, but there is internal disagreement on whether gender must be mentioned, and on how to handle mixed goods. We also learn additional conditions for claims related to deposit and usurpation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Primary Condition
Read the first sentence: the text states that for a claim of value of a consumed item, the plaintiff must specify the genus (jins) and type (naw'). This is the baseline requirement.
This is agreed upon.
2
Note the Ikhtilaf on Gender
The text mentions that scholars differed on whether to specify the gender of an animal. This dispute is recorded in al-Khazanah and Jami' al-Fusulayn.
Not settled.
3
Analyze the Dispute on Mixed Goods
Al-Bazzaziyyah holds that a claim for value of mixed items is invalid without specifying the items, because they might be mithli. Al-Nisab says no need, as the apparent is the demand of the obligatory.
Two valid views.
4
Examine the Additional Condition on Possession
Some mashayikh require stating that the taking was without permission, while others deem it unnecessary.
Minor ikhtilaf.
5
Understand Rules for Deposit and Usurpation
For deposit, the place must be specified always. For usurpation, it depends on whether the item has weight and maintenance.
Separate conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما يكتفى بالقيمة عند التعذر فيما إذا ادعى العين، أما إذا ادعى قيمة شيء مستهلك فلا بد من بيان جنسه ونوعه واختلفوا في بيان الذكورة والأنوثة في الدابة كما في الخزانة وجامع الفصولين. وفي البزازية : ودعوى قيمة الأعيان المشتركة لا تصح بلا بيان الأعيان لجواز أن يكون مثلياً ويطالب بالقيمة . وقال في النصاب : لا يحتاج إلى ذكر الأعيان لأن الظاهر المطالبة بالواجب فلا ترد الدعوى بالاحتمال قال بعض المشايخ: لا بد أن يذكر أن القبض كان بغير إذن المالك أو بغير حق، وقيل لا حاجة إليه لإغناء الطلب عن ذلك اهـ . ولم يفرق المؤلف بين دعوى عين وعين مع أن دعوى بعض الأعيان له شرط آخر . قال في البزازية: وفي دعوى الإيداع لا بد من بيان مكان الإيداع، سواء كان له حمل أو لا، وفي الغصب إن كان له حمل ومؤنة لا يصح بلا بيان المكان وإن لم يكن له حمل يصح ا هـ .
He only suffices with the value when [the thing] is unavailable in the case where he claims the [specific] item. However, if he claims the value of a consumed item, it is necessary to specify its genus and type, and they differed regarding specifying the masculinity and femininity of an animal as in al-Khazanah and Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: The claim for the value of mixed items is not valid without specifying the items because it is possible that it is a fungible (mithli) and he demands value. And it is said in al-Nisab: There is no need to mention the items because the apparent is the demand of the obligatory, so the claim is not rejected due to probability. Some mashayikh said: It is necessary that he mention that the taking was without the owner's permission or without right. And it is said: There is no need for that because the demand suffices from that. End. And the author did not differentiate between a claim for an item and an item, even though the claim for some items has another condition. He said in al-Bazzaziyyah: Regarding the claim of deposit, it is necessary to specify the place of deposit, whether it has weight or not. And regarding usurpation, if it has weight and maintenance, it is not valid without specifying the place; and if it does not have weight, it is valid. End.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentator on the conditions for properly filing a claim in court. The text first addresses claims for the value of a consumed item (mustahlak). The author states that when the actual item is unavailable, one may claim its value, but must specify the item's genus (jins) and type (naw'). There is disagreement among Hanafi scholars on whether one must also specify the gender of an animal (e.g., male/female). The disagreement is recorded in al-Khazanah and Jami' al-Fusulayn. Next, the text quotes al-Bazzaziyyah on claims for the value of mixed goods (a'yan mushtarakah). One view holds that such a claim is invalid without specifying the items, because the goods might be fungible (mithli) and thus the claim should be for the specific item. Another view (from al-Nisab) says no need to specify, as the claim is for the obligatory value. Some mashayikh require the plaintiff to state that the taking was without permission or right, while others consider this unnecessary. The commentator then notes that the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) did not distinguish between different types of claims for items, although some have additional conditions. Finally, the text covers claims for deposit (ida') and usurpation (ghasb). For deposit, the place of deposit must be specified regardless of weight. For usurpation, if the item has weight and requires maintenance (haml wa mu'nah), the place must be specified; otherwise, it is not required.

Key Terms
قيمة (qīmah): Monetary value or compensation for a lost or consumed item when the item itself is unavailable.
مستهلك (mustahlak): A consumed item that no longer exists, such as food eaten or destroyed property.
مثلي (mithlī): A fungible item that can be replaced by similar items of the same type and quantity.
إيداع (īdā‘): Deposit; entrusting property to another for safekeeping.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; wrongful taking of another's property without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazzaziyyah, why is a claim for value of mixed items invalid without specifying the items?
A) Because they might be mithli
B) Because they might be qimi
C) Because the value is unknown
D) Because the claim is incomplete

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires specification of genus and type for claims of value of consumed goods, with internal disputes on gender and mixed goods, and additional place conditions for deposit and usurpation claims.

Specifying Boundaries in Real Estate Claims
Ruling with proof on boundary specification for real estate claims, with intra-Hanafi difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on requiring boundaries in real estate claims and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on well-known property.

Core Idea
Real estate claims require boundary specification because physical pointing is impossible; even well-known property must be bounded according to Abu Hanifa.

In Islamic court procedure, immovable property (real estate) cannot be physically brought before the judge. Therefore, the claimant must describe its boundaries to identify it uniquely. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling and records an internal Hanafi difference: does well-known property still require boundary description?

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that claiming real estate in court requires mentioning boundaries. This is a wajib procedural requirement.
The ruling is derived from the impossibility of transporting the property for physical identification.
2
Understand the dalil aqli
The text states 'because definition by pointing is not possible due to the impossibility of transport'. This rational proof shows why description is necessary.
Unlike movable goods, real estate cannot be brought to court.
3
Note the exception
If witnesses know the property absolutely by sight, they may testify without mentioning boundaries. This is reported from al-Multaqit.
The exception applies when the property is uniquely identifiable by the witnesses.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa requires boundaries for all real estate, even if well-known. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not require it if the property is well-known. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
The commentator says 'contrary to them' indicating the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن ادعى عقاراً ذكر حدوده لأنه تعذر التعريف بالإشارة لتعذر النقل فيصار إلى التحديد، وكما يشترط التحديد في الدعوى يشترط في الشهادة وفي الملتقط : وإذا عرف الشهود الدار بعينها جاز وإن لم يذكروا حدودها .ا.هـ. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان العقار مشهوراً فلا بد من تحديده عنده خلافاً لهما كذا في منية المفتي : ولم يشترط المؤلف لدعوى العقار غير التحديد وفي جامع الفصولين في دعوى العقار: لا بد أن يذكر بلدة فيها الدار ثم المحلة ثم السكة فيبدأ أولاً بذكر الكورة ثم المحلة اختياراً لقول محمد فإن مذهبه أن يبدأ أولاً بالأعم ثم بالأخص فالأخص . وقيل : يبدأ بالأخص ثم بالأعم فيقول دار في سكة كذا في محلة كذا في كورة كذا، وقاسه على النسب فيقال فلان ثم يقال ابن فلان ثم يذكر الجد فبدأ بما هو أقرب فيترقى إلى الأبعد وقول محمد أحسن إذ العام يعرف بالخاص لا بالعكس وفصل النسب حجة عليه إذ الأعم اسمه فإن أحمد في الدنيا كثير فإن عرف وإلا ترقى إلى الأخص فيقول ابن محمد فإن عرف وإلا ترقى إلى الجد ا هـ . ثم قال : يكتب في الحد ثم ينتهي إلى كذا أيضاً.
His statement: (And if he claims real estate, he mentions its boundaries because definition by pointing is not possible due to the impossibility of transport, so one resorts to specification. And just as specification is required in the claim, it is required in testimony. And in al-Multaqit: If the witnesses know the house by its very self, it is permissible even if they do not mention its boundaries. End. He made it absolute, so it includes if the real estate is well-known – then it must be specified according to him, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Likewise in Munyat al-Mufti: And the author did not stipulate for claiming real estate other than specification. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn regarding claiming real estate: It is necessary to mention the town in which the house is located, then the district, then the alley. So one begins first with the region, then the district, as a choice according to the saying of Muhammad, for his madhhab is to begin first with the general, then the specific, then the more specific. And it is said: He begins with the specific then the general, saying: a house in such-and-such alley, in such-and-such district, in such-and-such region. And he analogized it to lineage: one says 'so-and-so', then 'son of so-and-so', then mentions the grandfather, so he begins with what is closer and ascends to the farther. The saying of Muhammad is better because the general is known by the specific, not vice versa, and the detailing of lineage is a proof against him, for the general is his name; indeed, Ahmad in the world is many. If he is known [by name], [fine]; otherwise, he ascends to the more specific saying 'son of Muhammad'; if he is known [by that], [fine]; otherwise, he ascends to the grandfather. End. Then he said: He writes about the boundary then ends at such-and-such as well.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the procedure for claiming real estate in court. The commentator explains the matn: if someone claims real estate (e.g., land or a house), they must mention its boundaries because, unlike movable property, real estate cannot be brought to court for physical identification. Therefore, written boundaries substitute for physical pointing. This requirement also applies to witnesses testifying about real estate. However, an exception is mentioned from al-Multaqit: if witnesses know the property absolutely by sight, they may testify without mentioning boundaries. The commentator then notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, even well-known property requires boundary specification, unlike his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). This is recorded in Munyat al-Mufti. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not require anything beyond specification for real estate claims. The text then quotes Jami' al-Fusulayn on the proper order of description: one should start with the broadest location (e.g., region or town), then narrow down to district, then alley. This is the preferred method of Imam Muhammad, who argues that the general is known by the specific, not vice versa. An alternative view is to start with the most specific and move outward, analogous to lineage description. Imam Muhammad's method is considered superior because it is more logical: one identifies the general category first, then restricts. The passage concludes with a technical point about writing boundaries: the document should record the boundaries and then end with 'up to such-and-such'.

Key Terms
عقار (‘iqār): Real estate; immovable property such as land, houses, and palm trees.
تحديد (taḥdīd): Specification of boundaries; describing the four sides of a property to identify it.
شهادة (shahādah): Testimony in court; witnessing.
كورة (kūrah): Region or district; a large administrative area.
محلة (maḥallah): Neighborhood or quarter within a town.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must boundaries be mentioned when claiming real estate?
A) Because it is a sunnah
B) Because real estate cannot be physically brought to court
C) Because the judge needs to know the area
D) Because witnesses require it

Key Takeaway
Specifying boundaries is wajib in real estate claims; even well-known property must be described according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

The Proper Order of Locality Description in Real Estate Claims
Procedure for describing locality of real estate in court claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi procedure for describing the location of a property in a court claim, specifying the order of generality preferred by Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
When describing real estate, start with the general (region) then move to the specific (district, alley) to ensure clarity.

Once boundaries are required, how should a claimant describe the property's location? The Hanafi jurists debated whether to begin with the broadest locality or the most specific. This passage presents Imam Muhammad's preferred method: from general to specific, analogizing to lineage description.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two methods
Identify the two orders: starting with the general (kurah, then mahallah, then sikkah) versus starting with the specific (sikkah, then mahallah, then kurah).
The text says 'He begins first with the region, then the district' as a choice for Muhammad.
2
Understand the analogy to lineage
In lineage, one typically starts with the specific person and goes back to the general ancestor. But Imam Muhammad argues the opposite: start with the general name (e.g., Ahmad) then specify father, then grandfather.
The analogy shows that the general is known by the specific only after the general is established.
3
Apply the preferred method
In practice, describe the property starting with the broadest locality, then narrow down. Example: 'House in Kufa, district al-Hira, alley Bani Tamim.'
This is considered better because the general context helps identify the specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن ادعى عقاراً ذكر حدوده لأنه تعذر التعريف بالإشارة لتعذر النقل فيصار إلى التحديد، وكما يشترط التحديد في الدعوى يشترط في الشهادة وفي الملتقط : وإذا عرف الشهود الدار بعينها جاز وإن لم يذكروا حدودها .ا.هـ. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان العقار مشهوراً فلا بد من تحديده عنده خلافاً لهما كذا في منية المفتي : ولم يشترط المؤلف لدعوى العقار غير التحديد وفي جامع الفصولين في دعوى العقار: لا بد أن يذكر بلدة فيها الدار ثم المحلة ثم السكة فيبدأ أولاً بذكر الكورة ثم المحلة اختياراً لقول محمد فإن مذهبه أن يبدأ أولاً بالأعم ثم بالأخص فالأخص . وقيل : يبدأ بالأخص ثم بالأعم فيقول دار في سكة كذا في محلة كذا في كورة كذا، وقاسه على النسب فيقال فلان ثم يقال ابن فلان ثم يذكر الجد فبدأ بما هو أقرب فيترقى إلى الأبعد وقول محمد أحسن إذ العام يعرف بالخاص لا بالعكس وفصل النسب حجة عليه إذ الأعم اسمه فإن أحمد في الدنيا كثير فإن عرف وإلا ترقى إلى الأخص فيقول ابن محمد فإن عرف وإلا ترقى إلى الجد ا هـ . ثم قال : يكتب في الحد ثم ينتهي إلى كذا أيضاً.

And it is said: He begins with the specific then the general, saying: a house in such-and-such alley, in such-and-such district, in such-and-such region. And he analogized it to lineage: one says 'so-and-so', then 'son of so-and-so', then mentions the grandfather, so he begins with what is closer and ascends to the farther. The saying of Muhammad is better because the general is known by the specific, not vice versa, and the detailing of lineage is a proof against him, for the general is his name; indeed, Ahmad in the world is many. If he is known [by name], [fine]; otherwise, he ascends to the more specific saying 'son of Muhammad'; if he is known [by that], [fine]; otherwise, he ascends to the grandfather. End. Claim Without Knowing Value: Acceptance and Benefit
Ruling on accepting a claim with unknown value and its procedural outcome.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting a claim when the claimant does not know the value of the item and explain the procedural benefit (fā'idah) of such acceptance.

Core Idea
A claim is valid even if the value is unknown because forcing disclosure would cause undue hardship.

In judicial proceedings, a claimant must normally specify the value of what he claims. However, Hanafi law recognizes that a person may not always know the exact value of his property. This lesson examines the ruling from al-Kafi: if someone says, 'Someone usurped from me such-and-such item, and I do not know whether it is destroyed or existing, nor its value,' the claim is still heard. Why? And what legal benefit does this serve? Let's explore.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Locate the key statement: the claim is heard (‘tusma‘ da‘wāhu) despite ignorance of value.
The ruling is found at the beginning of the passage from al-Durar quoting al-Kafi.
2
Understand the Rationale
The reason given is that requiring the claimant to know the exact value would cause harm (yataḍarru). This is a principle of easing hardship.
The harm (ḍarar) is the basis for leniency.
3
Identify the Benefit (Fā'idah)
The benefit of accepting such a vague claim is that it directs an oath (tawajjuh al-yamīn) against the defendant, compelling him to disclose or confess. If the defendant denies, he must take an oath; if he confesses or refuses, the court compels explanation.
The benefit is procedural, not substantive.
4
Distinguish Between Evidence and No Evidence
If the claimant has evidence (bayyinah), the benefit is imprisonment (ḥabs) of the defendant until he clarifies. Without evidence, the benefit is the oath.
The commentator clarifies this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الدرر قال في الكافي : وإن لم يبين القيمة وقال غصبت مني عين كذا ولا أدري أهو هالك أو قائم ولا أدري كم كانت قيمته ذكر في عامة الكتب أنه تسمع دعواه لأن الإنسان ربما لا يعلم قيمة ما له فلو كلف بيان القيمة لتضرر به أقول : فائدة صحة الدعوى هذه الجهالة الفاحشة مع توجه اليمين على الخصم إذا أنكر والجبر على البيان إذا أقر أو نكل عن اليمين فليتأمل، فإن كلام الكافي لا يكون كافياً إلا بهذا التحقيق .اهـ. وقوله «فائدتها توجه اليمين» أي حيث لا بينة وإلا ففائدتها الحبس كما علمت قوله : ( وإن لم يكن له حمل يصح قال في نور العين بعد هذه العبارة : وفي غصب غير المثلي وإهلاكه ينبغي أن يبين قيمته يوم غصبه في ظاهر الرواية، وفي رواية يتخير المالك أخذ قيمته غصبه أو هلاكه فلا بد من بيان أنها قيمة أي اليومين، ولو ادعى ألف دينار بسبب إهلاك الأعيان لا بد من أن يبين قيمتها في موضع الإهلاك، وكذا لا بد من بيان الأعيان فإن أو يلاصق كذا أو لزيق كذا ولا يكتب أحد حدوده كذا. وقال أبو حنيفة : لو كتب أحد حدوده دجلة أو الطريق أو المسجد فالبيع جائز ولا تدخل الحدود في البيع إذ قصد الناس به إظهار ما يقع عليه البيع لكن قال أبو يوسف البيع فاسد إذ الحدود فيه تدخل في البيع أو لزيق أو يلاصق تحرزاً عن الخلاف، ولأن الدار على قول من يقول يدخل فاخترنا ينتهي الحد لا يبين : الطريق يصلح حداً ولا حاجة فيه إلى بيان طوله وعرضه إلا على قول فإنه شرط أن يبينها بالذرع، والنهر لا يصلح حداً عند البعض وكذا السور وهو رواية عن أي حنيفة، وظاهر المذهب أنه يصلح حداً. والخندق كنهر ولو حد بأنه لزيق أرض فلان ولفلان في هذه القرية التي فيها المدعاة أراض كثيرة متفرقة مختلفة تصح الدعوى والشهادة ثم قال : لا بد من تحديد المستثنيات من المساجد والمقابر والحياض العامة لتتميز وما يكتبون في زماننا وقد عرف المتعاقدان جميع ذلك وأحاطا به علماً فقد استرذله بعض مشايخنا وهو المختار إذ المبيع يصير به معلوماً للقاضي عند الشهادة فلا بد من التعيين ا هـ . ثم قال: بين حدوده ولم أنه كرم أو أرض أو دار وشهدا كذلك، وقيل لا تسمع الدعوى ولا الشهادة، وقيل تسمع ولو بين المصر والمحلة والموضع ثم قال : ادعى سكنى دار و نحوه وبين حدوده لا يصح إذ السكنى نقلي فلا يحد بشيء، وإن كان السكنى نقلياً لكن لما اتصل بالأرض اتصال تأبيد كان تعريفه بما به تعريف الأرض إذ في سائر النقليات إنما لا يعرف بالحدود لإمكان إحضاره فيستغني بالإشارة إليه عن الحد أما السكنى فنقله لا يمكن لأنه مركب في البناء تركيب قرار فالتحق بما لا يمكن نقله أصلا شرى علو بيت ليس له سفل يحد السفل لا العلو إذ السفل مبيع من وجه من حيث إن قرار العلو عليه فلا بد من تحديده وتحديده يغني عن تحديد العلو إذ العلو عرف بتحديد السفل ولأن السفل أصل والعلو تبع فتحديد الأصل أولى. هذا إذا لم يكن حول العلو حجرة، فلو كانت ينبغي أن يحد العلو لأنه المبيع فلا بد من إعلامه وهو بحده وقد أمكن ا هـ . وفي المصباح: العقار كسلام كل ملك ثابت له أصل كالدار والنخل، وربما أطلق على المتاع والجمع عقارات ا هـ . . وفي المغرب : العقار الضيعة وقيل كل مال له أصل كالدار والضيعة ا هـ . وفي جامع الفصولين : ادعى هو منها ما هو قيمي ومنها ما هو مثلي اهـ وهذا ما ذكره المؤلف آنفاً عن البزازية قوله : (ثم قال ادعى سکنی دار) ضمیر «قال لصاحب جامع الفصولين والمراد بالسكنى ما ركب في الأرض كما يظهر مما بعده. وقوله «إن كان السكنى نقلياً «الخ هذا قول آخر رمز له في جامع الفصولين (فش) بعلامة
And in al-Durar, he said in al-Kafi: If he does not clarify the value and says 'Someone usurped from me such-and-such item, and I do not know whether it is destroyed or existing, and I do not know how much its value was,' it is mentioned in most books that his claim is heard because a person may not know the value of his property. If he were required to clarify the value, he would be harmed. I say: The benefit of the validity of such a claim is this manifest ignorance with the direction of the oath against the opponent if he denies, and compulsion to explain if he confesses or refuses the oath. So contemplate, for the words of al-Kafi are not sufficient except with this verification. End. And his statement: 'Its benefit is the direction of the oath' means when there is no evidence; otherwise, its benefit is imprisonment as you know. His statement: (And if it does not have weight, it is valid). He said in Nur al-‘Ayn after this phrase: Regarding the usurpation of a non-fungible (ghayr al-mithli) and its destruction, he should clarify its value on the day of its usurpation according to the apparent narration. In another narration, the owner has the option to take its value at the time of usurpation or at the time of destruction, so it is necessary to clarify that it is the value of either of the two days. And if he claims a thousand dinars due to the destruction of items, he must clarify their value at the place of destruction, and likewise he must clarify the items. Then: or it adjoins such-and-such or abuts such-and-such, and he does not write one of its boundaries. Abu Hanifa said: If one writes its boundaries as the Tigris, a road, or a mosque, the sale is valid, and the boundaries are not included in the sale because people intend by that to show what the sale covers. But Abu Yusuf said the sale is invalid because boundaries are included in the sale, or 'abuts' or 'adjoins' to avoid disagreement. And because the house, according to the one who says it enters, we choose that the boundary ends, he does not clarify: The road is valid as a boundary and there is no need to clarify its length and width except according to one saying, for it is required to specify them by cubits. The river is not valid as a boundary according to some, and likewise the wall; this is a narration from Abu Hanifa, and the apparent madhhab is that it is valid as a boundary. A moat is like a river. And if he specifies that it abuts the land of so-and-so in this village where there are many different scattered lands, the claim and testimony are valid. Then he said: It is necessary to specify the exceptions, such as mosques, cemeteries, and public watering places, so that they are distinguished. And what they write in our time, when the contracting parties know all that and are fully aware of it, some of our mashayikh have considered it base, and the chosen view is that the sale becomes known to the judge at testimony, so specification is necessary. End. Then he said: He specified its boundaries and did not clarify whether it is a vineyard or land or a house, and they testified likewise. It is said that neither the claim nor the testimony is heard. And it is said that it is heard, even if he specified the city, district, and location. Then he said: He claimed residence (sukna) of a house and the like, and specified its boundaries; it is not valid because sukna is movable (naqli) and is not defined by boundaries. Even though sukna is movable, when it is attached to the land with permanent attachment, its definition is by what defines land, because in other movables, they are only not defined by boundaries due to the possibility of bringing them, so one suffices with pointing to them instead of boundaries. As for sukna, its transfer is not possible because it is built into the structure with permanent placement, so it is treated as something that cannot be transferred at all. If he buys an upper room (uluww) without a lower part (sufl), he specifies the sufl, not the uluww, because the sufl is sold in a sense, since the uluww rests upon it, so it must be specified, and its specification suffices for specifying the uluww because the uluww is known by the specification of the sufl, and because the sufl is the origin and the uluww is a follower, so specifying the origin takes precedence. This is if there is no room around the upper room. If there is, he should specify the upper room because it is the sold item, so it must be identified by its boundary, and it is possible. End. And in al-Misbah: 'Aqar: like 'salam', every property that has a foundation like a house and palm trees, and sometimes it is used for furnishings; plural 'aqarat. End. And in al-Mughrib: 'Aqar: a landed estate (day‘ah), and it is said: every property that has a foundation like a house and estate. End. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He claimed... among them are what is qimi and among them are what is mithli. End. This is what the author mentioned earlier from al-Bazzaziyyah. His statement: (Then he said: He claimed residence of a house) – the pronoun 'he said' refers to the author of Jami' al-Fusulayn, and what is meant by sukna is what is built into the ground, as appears from what follows. And his statement: 'If sukna is movable...' etc. This is another saying indicated in Jami' al-Fusulayn with the symbol (f-sh).
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a complex collection of rulings and disputes within the Hanafi school, covering several topics. It begins with a discussion from al-Kafi: if a person claims that someone usurped an item and he does not know its value or whether it still exists, the claim is still accepted in court because requiring him to know the exact value would cause hardship. The benefit (fā'idah) of accepting such a vague claim is that it directs an oath to the defendant and compels him to reveal information. The commentator adds that this ruling applies only when there is no evidence; with evidence, the benefit is imprisonment. Next, the text addresses usurpation of non-fungible goods (ghayr al-mithli): the plaintiff must specify the value on the day of usurpation according to the apparent narration (zāhir al-riwāyah), but a minority narration gives the owner a choice between value at usurpation or destruction. For a claim of a thousand dinars due to destruction of items, he must specify the value at the place and time of destruction, and the items themselves. The passage then shifts to boundaries in sales. Abu Hanifa holds that if a boundary is written as a river, road, or mosque, the sale is valid and the boundary itself is not included in the sale because the intention is merely to identify the property. Abu Yusuf disagrees, considering the sale invalid because boundaries enter the sale. The majority view is that boundaries like roads or rivers are valid identifiers, and there is no need to specify length and width except in one opinion that requires cubit measurements. A river is not a valid boundary according to some, including a narration from Abu Hanifa, but the apparent position of the school is that it is valid. A moat is treated like a river. If a boundary is given as 'abutting the land of so-and-so' in a village with many scattered plots, the claim and testimony are valid. Exceptions like mosques, cemeteries, and public water sources must be specifically identified to be excluded. Some later authorities criticize modern practices where boundaries are omitted if both parties know the property, but the chosen view (al-mukhtār) is that specification is needed for the judge. The passage concludes with a detailed discussion on claiming usufructuary rights like sukna (right of residence). Sukna is considered movable (naqlī) but because it is permanently attached to land, it must be identified by boundaries (like land) rather than by pointing. If someone buys an upper room (uluww) without the lower part (sufl), he must specify the sufl because the uluww rests on it and the sufl is the origin. If there is a separate room on the upper floor, then the uluww itself must be specified. Finally, definitions of 'aqār are given from al-Misbah and al-Mughrib. The passage ends with a reference to Jami' al-Fusulayn and the commentary on sukna.

Key Terms
دعوى (da‘wā): A legal claim or lawsuit.
قيمة (qīmah): Value or price of an item.
يمين (yamīn): Oath taken by a party in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the benefit (fā'idah) of accepting a claim where the claimant does not know the value?
A) It allows the judge to estimate the value
B) It directs an oath to the defendant and compels disclosure
C) It automatically gives the claimant the requested amount
D) It delays the trial until the value is known

Key Takeaway
A claim for usurpation is valid even if the value is unknown, and its procedural benefit is the direction of an oath to the defendant.

Qada — Judiciary

Boundaries in Sale: Abu Hanifa vs Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inclusion of boundaries in a sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding whether boundaries like river, road, or mosque are included in the sale, and state the chosen (mufta bihi) view.

Core Idea
Boundaries in a sale are for identification only; they are not sold unless explicitly included.

When selling a property, boundaries are often written in the contract. Do these boundaries become part of the sale? For example, if a house is sold with a boundary marked as 'river', does the buyer own part of the river? Abu Hanifa and his student Abu Yusuf disagreed on this point, leading to significant practical consequences. This lesson examines their arguments and the preferred position in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa holds that the sale is valid and boundaries like river, road, or mosque are not included. They are only for identifying what is sold.
The reasoning is that people intend by boundaries to show the scope, not to convey the boundaries themselves.
2
State Abu Yusuf's Position
Abu Yusuf considers the sale invalid unless the boundaries are explicitly excluded. He argues that boundaries are part of the sold property.
The practical outcome: according to Abu Yusuf, if you write 'bounded by the road', the road is sold with the house, making the sale ambiguous.
3
Identify the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer does not own the road. Under Abu Yusuf, the sale is invalid unless the road is excluded.
The judge must choose one view to apply.
4
Determine the Tarjih
The commentator states that the chosen view (fakhtarna) is that boundaries are not included. This is the mufta bihi position.
This follows Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a complex collection of rulings and disputes within the Hanafi school, covering several topics. It begins with a discussion from al-Kafi: if a person claims that someone usurped an item and he does not know its value or whether it still exists, the claim is still accepted in court because requiring him to know the exact value would cause hardship. Abu Yusuf disagrees, considering the sale invalid because boundaries enter the sale. The majority view is that boundaries like roads or rivers are valid identifiers, and there is no need to specify length and width except in one opinion that requires cubit measurements. A river is not a valid boundary according to some, including a narration from Abu Hanifa, but the apparent position of the school is that it is valid. A moat is treated like a river. If there is a separate room on the upper floor, then the uluww itself must be specified. Finally, definitions of 'aqār are given from al-Misbah and al-Mughrib. Key Terms
حد (ḥadd): Boundary line; limits of a property.
دجلة (Dijlah): The Tigris river.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; wrongful taking of property.

According to the mufta bihi view, if a house is sold with a boundary stating 'adjoins the mosque', does the buyer own part of the mosque?
A) Yes, because the boundary includes it.
B) No, the mosque is not part of the sale.
C) The sale is invalid.
D) It depends on the buyer's intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, boundaries written in a sale contract are for identification only and do not convey ownership of those boundaries.

Defining Usufructuary Rights: Sukna, Uluww, and Sufl
Ruling on the legal definition of usufructuary rights attached to land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a claim for sukna (right of residence) must be defined by boundaries like land, and how to specify the upper part (uluww) when the lower part (sufl) is not owned.

Core Idea
Although sukna is movable, its permanent attachment to land requires it to be identified by boundaries, not by pointing.

Usufructuary rights like sukna (right to reside) pose a unique challenge: they are legally movable (manqūl) but physically attached to real estate. How should they be described in court? This lesson explores the Hanafi rules for defining such rights, including the specification of upper and lower parts of a building.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Nature of Sukna
Sukna is a right of residence. It is considered movable (naqlī) because it is a usufruct, not the land itself. However, it is permanently attached to the building.
This hybrid nature leads to special rules for identification.
2
Apply the Rule for Movables vs Immovables
Normally, movables are identified by pointing (ishārah), not boundaries. But because sukna is fixed, it follows the rule for land: it must be defined by boundaries.
The text says: 'since it is attached with permanent attachment, its definition is by what defines land.'
3
Analyze the Case of Uluww (Upper Room) without Sufl
If someone buys an upper room (uluww) without the lower part, the sale includes the sufl in a sense because the upper room rests on it. So you specify the sufl, not the uluww.
The sufl is the origin (asl) and the uluww is a follower (tabi‘). Specifying the origin suffices.
4
Identify the Exception
If there is a separate room around the upper part (e.g., an independent room on the roof), then the uluww itself must be specified because it is the actual sold item and can be identified independently.
The text says: 'fa-law kānat yanbaghī an yuḥadda al-‘uluww li-annahu al-mabī‘'.

العلو إذ السفل مبيع من وجه من حيث إن قرار العلو عليه فلا بد من تحديده وتحديده يغني عن تحديد العلو إذ العلو عرف بتحديد السفل ولأن السفل أصل والعلو تبع فتحديد الأصل أولى. هذا إذا لم يكن حول العلو حجرة،
