العقار الضيعة وقيل كل مال له أصل كالدار والضيعة ا هـ . وفي جامع الفصولين : ادعى هو منها ما هو قيمي ومنها ما هو مثلي اهـ وهذا ما ذكره المؤلف آنفاً عن البزازية قوله : (ثم قال ادعى سکنی دار) ضمیر «قال لصاحب جامع الفصولين والمراد بالسكنى ما ركب في الأرض كما يظهر مما بعده. وقوله «إن كان السكنى نقلياً «الخ هذا قول آخر رمز له في جامع الفصولين (فش) بعلامة
And in al-Durar, he said in al-Kafi: If he does not clarify the value and says 'Someone usurped from me such-and-such item, and I do not know whether it is destroyed or existing, and I do not know how much its value was,' it is mentioned in most books that his claim is heard because a person may not know the value of his property. If he were required to clarify the value, he would be harmed. I say: The benefit of the validity of such a claim is this manifest ignorance with the direction of the oath against the opponent if he denies, and compulsion to explain if he confesses or refuses the oath. So contemplate, for the words of al-Kafi are not sufficient except with this verification. End. And his statement: 'Its benefit is the direction of the oath' means when there is no evidence; otherwise, its benefit is imprisonment as you know. His statement: (And if it does not have weight, it is valid). He said in Nur al-‘Ayn after this phrase: Regarding the usurpation of a non-fungible (ghayr al-mithli) and its destruction, he should clarify its value on the day of its usurpation according to the apparent narration. In another narration, the owner has the option to take its value at the time of usurpation or at the time of destruction, so it is necessary to clarify that it is the value of either of the two days. And if he claims a thousand dinars due to the destruction of items, he must clarify their value at the place of destruction, and likewise he must clarify the items. Then: or it adjoins such-and-such or abuts such-and-such, and he does not write one of its boundaries. Abu Hanifa said: If one writes its boundaries as the Tigris, a road, or a mosque, the sale is valid, and the boundaries are not included in the sale because people intend by that to show what the sale covers. But Abu Yusuf said the sale is invalid because boundaries are included in the sale, or 'abuts' or 'adjoins' to avoid disagreement. And because the house, according to the one who says it enters, we choose that the boundary ends, he does not clarify: The road is valid as a boundary and there is no need to clarify its length and width except according to one saying, for it is required to specify them by cubits. The river is not valid as a boundary according to some, and likewise the wall; this is a narration from Abu Hanifa, and the apparent madhhab is that it is valid as a boundary. A moat is like a river. And if he specifies that it abuts the land of so-and-so in this village where there are many different scattered lands, the claim and testimony are valid. Then he said: It is necessary to specify the exceptions, such as mosques, cemeteries, and public watering places, so that they are distinguished. And what they write in our time, when the contracting parties know all that and are fully aware of it, some of our mashayikh have considered it base, and the chosen view is that the sale becomes known to the judge at testimony, so specification is necessary. End. Then he said: He specified its boundaries and did not clarify whether it is a vineyard or land or a house, and they testified likewise. It is said that neither the claim nor the testimony is heard. And it is said that it is heard, even if he specified the city, district, and location. Then he said: He claimed residence (sukna) of a house and the like, and specified its boundaries; it is not valid because sukna is movable (naqli) and is not defined by boundaries. Even though sukna is movable, when it is attached to the land with permanent attachment, its definition is by what defines land, because in other movables, they are only not defined by boundaries due to the possibility of bringing them, so one suffices with pointing to them instead of boundaries. As for sukna, its transfer is not possible because it is built into the structure with permanent placement, so it is treated as something that cannot be transferred at all. If he buys an upper room (uluww) without a lower part (sufl), he specifies the sufl, not the uluww, because the sufl is sold in a sense, since the uluww rests upon it, so it must be specified, and its specification suffices for specifying the uluww because the uluww is known by the specification of the sufl, and because the sufl is the origin and the uluww is a follower, so specifying the origin takes precedence. This is if there is no room around the upper room. If there is, he should specify the upper room because it is the sold item, so it must be identified by its boundary, and it is possible. End. And in al-Misbah: 'Aqar: like 'salam', every property that has a foundation like a house and palm trees, and sometimes it is used for furnishings; plural 'aqarat. End. And in al-Mughrib: 'Aqar: a landed estate (day‘ah), and it is said: every property that has a foundation like a house and estate. End. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He claimed... among them are what is qimi and among them are what is mithli. End. This is what the author mentioned earlier from al-Bazzaziyyah. His statement: (Then he said: He claimed residence of a house) – the pronoun 'he said' refers to the author of Jami' al-Fusulayn, and what is meant by sukna is what is built into the ground, as appears from what follows. And his statement: 'If sukna is movable...' etc. This is another saying indicated in Jami' al-Fusulayn with the symbol (f-sh).
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a complex collection of rulings and disputes within the Hanafi school, covering several topics. It begins with a discussion from al-Kafi: if a person claims that someone usurped an item and he does not know its value or whether it still exists, the claim is still accepted in court because requiring him to know the exact value would cause hardship. The benefit (fā'idah) of accepting such a vague claim is that it directs an oath to the defendant and compels him to reveal information. The commentator adds that this ruling applies only when there is no evidence; with evidence, the benefit is imprisonment. Next, the text addresses usurpation of non-fungible goods (ghayr al-mithli): the plaintiff must specify the value on the day of usurpation according to the apparent narration (zāhir al-riwāyah), but a minority narration gives the owner a choice between value at usurpation or destruction. For a claim of a thousand dinars due to destruction of items, he must specify the value at the place and time of destruction, and the items themselves. The passage then shifts to boundaries in sales. Abu Hanifa holds that if a boundary is written as a river, road, or mosque, the sale is valid and the boundary itself is not included in the sale because the intention is merely to identify the property. Abu Yusuf disagrees, considering the sale invalid because boundaries enter the sale. The majority view is that boundaries like roads or rivers are valid identifiers, and there is no need to specify length and width except in one opinion that requires cubit measurements. A river is not a valid boundary according to some, including a narration from Abu Hanifa, but the apparent position of the school is that it is valid. A moat is treated like a river. If a boundary is given as 'abutting the land of so-and-so' in a village with many scattered plots, the claim and testimony are valid. Exceptions like mosques, cemeteries, and public water sources must be specifically identified to be excluded. Some later authorities criticize modern practices where boundaries are omitted if both parties know the property, but the chosen view (al-mukhtār) is that specification is needed for the judge. The passage concludes with a detailed discussion on claiming usufructuary rights like sukna (right of residence). Sukna is considered movable (naqlī) but because it is permanently attached to land, it must be identified by boundaries (like land) rather than by pointing. If someone buys an upper room (uluww) without the lower part (sufl), he must specify the sufl because the uluww rests on it and the sufl is the origin. If there is a separate room on the upper floor, then the uluww itself must be specified. Finally, definitions of 'aqār are given from al-Misbah and al-Mughrib. The passage ends with a reference to Jami' al-Fusulayn and the commentary on sukna.

Key Terms
سكنى (suknā): Right of residence; a usufructuary right to live in a property.
علو (‘uluww): Upper storey or room.
سفل (sufl): Lower storey or ground floor.
نقلي (naqlī): Movable property; can be transferred physically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must a claim for sukna (right of residence) include property boundaries?
A) Because sukna is immovable property
B) Because sukna is movable but permanently attached, so boundaries are needed
C) Because the judge cannot see the property
D) Because the claimant must prove ownership of the land

Key Takeaway
Sukna and other attached usufructs must be defined by boundaries, and upper parts are usually defined by their lower counterparts.

Validity of a Claim When Boundaries Are Given But Property Type Is Not Stated
Ruling on claim validity with boundaries only

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on the admissibility of a claim when boundaries are specified but property type is not, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A claim specifying boundaries but not property type is subject to two Hanafi views: one rejects, one accepts.

This lesson deals with a specific scenario in qada (judicial procedure): when a claimant describes a property by its boundaries only, without stating whether it is a house, land, or vineyard. The classical Hanafi text presents two positions. Understanding this difference helps clarify how courts evaluate the sufficiency of property descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The claimant gives the boundaries of the property but does not mention what type of property it is (e.g., vineyard, land, house). Witnesses also testify only to boundaries.
The text says: 'He specified its boundaries but did not state whether it is a vineyard, land, or house.'
2
State the first opinion
Some said: the claim and testimony are not heard (lā tusma‘). This view requires a clearer description to avoid ambiguity.
‘Qīl lā tusma‘ al-da‘wā wa-lā al-shahāda.’
3
State the second opinion
Others said: they are heard, even if the description is very general, like 'between the city and the quarter.'
‘Qīl tusma‘ wa-law bayna al-miṣr wa-l-maḥalla wa-l-mawḍi‘.’
4
Note the outcome
The text does not clearly prefer one view, leaving it as a legitimate ikhtilāf within the Hanafi school. The muftā bihī is not specified here.
This is an expansion (sharḥ) presenting both positions without tarjīḥ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: بين حدوده ولم أنه كرم أو أرض أو دار وشهدا كذلك، وقيل لا تسمع الدعوى ولا الشهادة، وقيل تسمع ولو بين المصر والمحلة والموضع.
Then he said: He specified its boundaries but did not state whether it is a vineyard, land, or house, and they testified likewise. Some said the claim and testimony are not heard. Some said they are heard, even if between the city, the quarter, and the location.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This ruling discusses a claim where the claimant specifies the boundaries of a property but does not mention what type of property it is (e.g., vineyard, land, house). The witnesses also testify only to the boundaries. There are two opinions: one view holds that such a claim and testimony are not admissible because the subject matter is not sufficiently identified. The other view holds it is admissible, even if the description is very general like 'between the city and the quarter.' The text does not specify which is preferred, leaving it as a difference of opinion.

Key Terms
كرم (karm): Vineyard; a type of agricultural land.
مصر (misr): City or large town.
محلة (mahallah): Quarter or neighborhood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to one opinion in the text, a claim that specifies boundaries but not the type of property is:
A) Always rejected
B) Always accepted
C) There are two opinions: one rejects, one accepts
D) Requires additional description

Key Takeaway
There is a legitimate disagreement within the Hanafi school on whether a claim specifying only boundaries is admissible.

Claim of a Mill: The Requirement of Specificity
Ruling on the validity of claiming a mill when tools are mentioned but not specified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a claim for a mill with unspecified tools and identify the condition that makes the claim valid or invalid.

Core Idea
A claim must specify the claimed item; general mention of tools makes the claim invalid.

In court, the subject matter of a claim must be clearly identified. This passage from Jami' al-Fusulayn illustrates the principle using the example of a mill: if the claimant only says 'a mill with its tools' without naming them, is the claim valid? The Hanafi school has two views, and the more correct one holds that such ambiguity renders the claim invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed item and the issue
The claim is for a mill, but only general tools are mentioned. The issue is whether this is sufficiently specific to avoid ambiguity (gharar).
2
State the two opinions
One opinion says the claim is invalid due to lack of specification; the other says it is valid if all present tools are mentioned.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The text explicitly states 'al-awwal asahh' (the first is more correct), making the invalid opinion the relied-upon position.
4
Extract the underlying condition
A valid claim requires the claimed item to be ma'lum (known) in its type, quantity, and description. Here, the tools must be named and described.
5
Apply the condition
Since the claimant only mentioned general tools without specification, the claim fails to meet the condition of clarity, thus it is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين : ادعى طاحونة وحدها وذكر أداواتها العامة إلا أنه لم يسم الأدوات ولم يذكر كيفيتها فقد قيل لا تصح الدعوى، وقيل تصح إذا ذكر جميع ما فيها من الأدوات القائمة والأول أصح ا هـ .
And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He claimed a mill by itself and mentioned its general tools, but he did not name the tools nor describe their condition. It is said the claim is not valid. And it is said it is valid if he mentioned all the tools that are in it, and the first is more correct. Ends.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage from Jami' al-Fusulayn discusses a claim for a mill (tahuna) where the claimant only generally mentions that it has tools but does not specify them by name or description. There are two opinions: one holds the claim is invalid due to ambiguity, while the other says it is valid if he mentions all the tools that are present. The preferred view is the first (invalid), because the lack of specification prevents clear identification of the claimed item.

Key Terms
أداواتها العامة (adawātihā al-'āmmah): General tools of the mill, without specification.
الأدوات القائمة (al-adawāt al-qā'imah): The tools that are present and standing in the mill.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A claim for a composite object must specify each component; general mention of tools renders the claim invalid per the more correct Hanafi view.

Claiming the Price of a Sold Item: No Need to Specify Boundaries
Ruling on claim of price: boundaries not required; undelivered item must be brought to court.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between claims for a specific property and claims for a price (debt), and apply the rule regarding the necessity of bringing the sold item to court.

Core Idea
Claiming a price is a debt claim; boundaries are irrelevant. Only if the item is undelivered must it be produced.

This lesson clarifies a procedural point in Hanafi qada (judicial procedure): when a claimant seeks the price of a sold item, they need not describe the item's boundaries because the price is a debt. However, if the item has not yet been delivered, the item itself must be brought to court to establish the sale. This distinction prevents confusion between claims for specific property and claims for debt.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule
Identify that the passage addresses claiming the price (thaman) of a sold item. The key point: specifying boundaries (hudud) is not required because price is a debt claim.
This is similar to any debt claim.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Recognize the two scenarios: (1) the sold item has not been received (ma lam yuqbad) – then the item must be brought to court; (2) the item has been received (qabad) – then it is purely a debt claim and no need to bring the item.
The difference hinges on whether the item is still in the seller's possession.
3
Apply the legal rationale
The rationale: if the item is undelivered, the sale needs proof via the item itself; if delivered, the price is a pure debt and the court only needs evidence of the debt, not the item.
This follows the principle that a debt claim is separate from a property claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بدعوى المحدود إذ لو ادعى ثمن محدود لم يشترط بيان حدوده؛ كذا في السراجية. وفي جامع الفصولين: ولو ادعى ثمن مبيع لم يقبض لا بد من إحضار المبيع مجلس الحكم حتى يثبت البيع عند القاضي بخلاف ما لو ادعى ثمن مبيع قبض فإنه لا يجب إحضاره لأنه دعوى الدين حقيقة.
And it is restricted to the claim of a defined property, because if he claimed the price of a defined item, specifying its boundaries is not required; likewise in al-Sirajiyyah. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he claimed the price of a sold item that has not been received, it is necessary to bring the sold item to the court session so that the sale is proven to the judge, contrary to if he claimed the price of a sold item that has been received, for then its presence is not required because it is essentially a debt claim.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between claiming a property itself and claiming its price. When claiming the price (thaman) of a sold item, the claimant does not need to specify the boundaries of the item because the price is a debt. However, if the price is for an item that has not yet been delivered, the claimant must bring the item to court to prove the sale. But if the item has already been delivered, the claim is purely for debt, and no need to present the item. This is based on the principle that a claim for debt is different from a claim for a specific property.

Key Terms
الثمن (al-thaman): The price in a sale contract.
دعوى الدين (da'wa al-dayn): Claim for a debt; does not require specifying the original item.
المبيع (al-mabī'): The sold item.
القبض (al-qabḍ): Taking possession, delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a claim for the price of a sold item that has already been delivered, is it necessary to bring the item to court?
A) Yes, always
B) No, because it is a debt claim
C) Only if the seller demands
D) Only if the price is unspecified

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a claim for the price of a sold item is a debt claim; boundaries need not be specified, and if the item is delivered, the item itself need not be produced in court.

Requirements for Describing Real Estate in a Claim
Ruling on the necessity of specifying names and boundaries in a claim for real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for describing real estate in a claim and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the necessity of specifying boundaries for famous properties.

Core Idea
In a claim for real estate, boundaries must be specified even if the property is famous — Abu Hanifa's view prevails over the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines the Hanafi requirements for describing real estate in a judicial claim. The plaintiff must specify the property's owners, their lineages, and boundaries to avoid ambiguity. The text also addresses the treatment of waqf land and the effect of incorrect descriptions in witness testimony. Understanding these conditions is essential for any student of Hanafi qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'describing real estate in a claim'. The ruling is wajib: mention owners, names, lineages, and boundaries.
This is a condition for the validity of the claim.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas: specificity prevents ambiguity and harm.
No direct nass is cited; the reasoning is based on the principle of avoiding gharar.
3
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa requires boundaries even for famous properties; Sahibayn accept fame as sufficient.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
4
Apply to waqf land
If the boundary adjoins waqf land, the plaintiff must mention the waqf founder and beneficiary.
This additional specificity is needed due to the nature of waqf.
5
Understand the effect of error
If the defendant admits ownership but later claims boundary error, the admission binds him; if he denies ownership, he may later raise error.
Witness testimony with incorrect quantity is rejected according to the stronger opinion.

قوله: (وأسماء أصحابها أي إن كان المدعي عقاراً ذكر أسماء أصحابها لأن التعريف يحصل بذلك وأسماء أنسابهم ليتميزوا عن غيرهم. ا هـ. قوله : ( ولا بد من ذكر الجدان لم يكن مشهوراً لأن تمام التعريف به فإن كان مشهوراً اكتفى بذكره وقدمنا أنه لا يكتفى بشهرة الدار عن تحديدها عنده خلافاً لهما. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الحد لزيق أرض وقف فلا بد من ذكر الواقف ،وحده ولا بد أن يذكر المصرف وأن يذكر أنه في يد من و لو قال على مسجد كذا يجوز ويكون كذكر الواقف . وقيل : لا . ولو (طه) فينتفي ما ذكره المدعي ضمناً فيكون شهادة على الإثبات لا على النفي، ويدل عليه مسألة ذكرت في فصل التناقض أنه ادعى داراً محدودة فأجاب المدعى عليه أنه ملكي وفي يدي، ثم ادعى أن المدعي غلط في بعض حدوده لم يسمع لأن جوابه إقرار بأنه بهذه الحدود وهذا إذا أجاب بأنه ملكي، لو أجاب بقوله ليس هذا ملكك ولم يزد عليه يمكن الدفع بعده بخطأ الحدود؛ كذا حكى عن أنه لقن المدعى عليه الدفع بخطأ الحدود أقول دل هذا على أن المدعى عليه لو برهن على الغلط يقبل فدل على ضعف الجوابين المذكورين، فالحق ما قلت أنه من ينبغي أن يكون على التفصيل والله تعالى أعلم اهـ. قال في نور العين جميع ما ذكره المعترض في هذا البحث محل نظر كما لا يخفى على من تأمل وتدبر. قوله : (ثم قال ولو شهد الخ) أقول : عبارة جامع الفصولين : شهدا بملكية أرض وحداه وقالا هو بمقدار خمسة مكاييل بذر والمدعي يدعي ذلك وأصابوا في الحد لا المقدار فظهر أنه يسع فيه ثلاثة مكاييل بذر، قيل ترد وهو الأظهر والأشبه بالفقه، وقيل تقبل إذ بيان القدر لا يحتاج إليه فصار ذكره وعدمه سواء. ونص في السير الكبير أن ذكر الشاهد في شهادته ما لا يحتاج إليه للحكم بالمشهود به ولا ذكره سواء، وقيل لو شهد بحضرة الأرض وأشار إليه يقبل ويلغو ذكر الوصف وهو قدر البذر ، ولو شهدا بغيبة الأرض لا تثبت بشهادتهما ملكية أرض يسع فيه خمسة مکاییل بذر. أقول : قد مر أن الوصف في الإشارة لغو في البيع والأثمان، أما في باب الشهادة لو شهدا بوصف فظهر خلافه لا يقبل الخ. وهذا يخالف القولين الأخيرين فظهر أن في باب الشهادة اختلافاً .اهـ.
His statement: (And the names of their owners) i.e., if the claim is for real estate, he mentions the names of its owners because identification is achieved thereby, and the names of their lineages to differentiate them from others. End. His statement: (And it is necessary to mention the grandfather if not well-known) because complete identification is through it; if it is well-known, its mention suffices. And we have previously stated that the fame of a house does not suffice over its specification according to him [Abu Hanifa], contrary to the two companions. He made it absolute, so it includes when the boundary is adjacent to waqf land—then the mention of the waqf founder is obligatory, and his boundary, and he must mention the beneficiary, and that it is in the possession of someone. If he says 'on a mosque such-and-such,' it is permissible and counts as mentioning the founder. And it is said: no. And if (something) then what the plaintiff mentioned is implicitly negated, so it becomes testimony for affirmation, not negation. This is indicated by a case mentioned in the chapter of contradiction: he claimed a bounded house, the defendant answered 'it is my property and in my possession,' then he claimed that the plaintiff erred in some of its boundaries—this is not heard, because his answer is an admission that it is with these boundaries. This is when he answered 'it is my property.' If he answered by saying 'this is not your property' and added nothing, he can later raise the error of boundaries. Thus it is related that he was prompted to defend by error of boundaries. I say: This indicates that if the defendant proves the error, it is accepted, showing the weakness of the two mentioned answers. So the correct is what I said: it should be detailed, and God knows best. End. He said in Nur al-'Ayn: All that the objector mentioned in this investigation is subject to consideration, as is not hidden from one who reflects and ponders. His statement: (Then he said, and if they testify...). I say: The phrase in Jami' al-Fusulayn: Two witnesses testified to ownership of land and its boundaries, and said it is the size of five kayl of seed, and the plaintiff claims that, and they were correct in the boundary but not the quantity, and it appeared that it contains three kayl of seed. It was said: reject, and that is the more apparent and more fitting to fiqh; and it was said: accept, because mentioning the size is not needed, so its mention or omission is same. And it is stated in al-Siyar al-Kabir that a witness mentioning in his testimony what is not needed for the judgment of what is testified to is like not mentioning it. And it was said: if he testifies in the presence of the land and points to it, it is accepted and the description is disregarded, meaning the seed quantity. If they testify in the absence of the land, ownership of land containing five kayl of seed is not established by their testimony. I say: It has passed that the description in pointing is null in sale and price; as for the chapter of testimony, if they testify with a description and it appears contrary, it is not accepted. This contradicts the last two opinions, so it is clear that there is difference in the chapter of testimony. End.)
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage details the requirements for describing real estate in a claim. The plaintiff must name the owners and their lineages to uniquely identify the property. If the property is not well-known, the grandfather's name must be mentioned; well-known properties are excepted. Abu Hanifa insists on boundary specification even for famous houses, unlike his two companions. When the boundary adjoins waqf land, one must mention the founder and the beneficiary. If the boundary is a mosque, it suffices to mention the mosque's name. The text then discusses a case where the defendant initially admits ownership but later claims an error in boundaries—the claim is not heard because the admission binds him. However, if the defendant simply denies ownership, he may later raise the error. The correct view is that the defendant's claim of error should be accepted if proven. Finally, it treats the case of witnesses testifying to land with an incorrect quantity; the stronger opinion is to reject the testimony since the size does not match, though some accept it because size is not essential. The passage notes a difference of opinion within the school on relying on descriptions in testimony.

Key Terms
جد (jadd): The grandfather (paternal lineage) used for identification in a claim.
وقف (waqf): Endowment; immovable property dedicated to charitable purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a plaintiff claims ownership of a famous house, must he specify its boundaries?
A) No, fame suffices
B) Yes, boundaries are always required
C) Only if the house is adjacent to waqf land
D) It depends on the judge's discretion

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the description of real estate in a claim must include owners, lineages, and boundaries; fame does not excuse boundary specification per Abu Hanifa's relied-upon view.

Qada — Judiciary

Proving Possession in Immovable Property: Conditions and Exceptions
Commentary on the requirement of evidence for possession in immovable property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that possession in immovable property requires evidence or judge's knowledge, and distinguish when such proof is necessary based on the type of claim (absolute ownership vs. specific cause).

Core Idea
Immovable property possession cannot be established by mutual agreement; evidence is required to prevent collusion.

In court claims over property, establishing who holds possession is often the first step. But Hanafi law treats immovable and movable property differently: you cannot simply agree on possession of land or buildings; you must bring proof. Why? Because two people might collude to fraudulently transfer ownership. This lesson unpacks the rule, its exceptions, and a critical intra-Hanafi dispute about witness testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on possession in immovable vs. movable property
Locate the statement in the source: possession in immovable property is not established by mutual agreement but by evidence or judge's knowledge. Contrast with movable property, where mutual agreement suffices.
The ruling is procedural, not substantive ownership.
2
Explain the rationale: tuhmat al-muwada'a (suspicion of collusion)
Understand why immovable property is singled out: because the owner is often not physically present, parties might collude by one falsely admitting possession and the other claiming ownership with lenient witnesses.
This is an example of sad al-dhara'i (blocking the means to wrongdoing).
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the witness phrase
Some scholars require witnesses to explicitly state that the property is in the defendant's hand 'without right' (bi-ghayr haqq). The mufta bihi is that testimony is accepted for ownership even without this phrase, but for delivery of the property, it is required.
This is a difference within the Hanafi school, not between madhahib.
4
Distinguish claims of absolute ownership from claims based on a specific cause
The requirement to prove possession applies only to claims of absolute ownership (da'wa al-milk al-mutlaq). In claims of usurpation (ghasb) or purchase (shira'), the plaintiff does not need to prove the defendant's possession because the claim is about a past action that could involve anyone.
In claims of action, the plaintiff can sue even a non-possessor.
5
Evaluate the Farghana regional opinion and its tarjih
Scholars of Farghana required specifying the cause of ownership in old cities where original owners no longer exist. The majority does not adopt this view, considering it a regional exception.
The mufta bihi is that absolute ownership claims are valid without specifying the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاضي بخلاف المنقول وأنه يطالبه وإن كان ديناً ذكر وصفه وأنه يطالبه به فإذا صحت يشترط أن يبين كون جميع الدار في يد المدعى عليه؟ قيل يشترط إذ غصب نصفه شائعاً لا يكون إلا بكون كله بيده، وقيل غصب نصفه شائعاً يتصور بأن تكون الدار بينهما فغصب من أحدهما يكون غصباً لنصفه شائعاً ا.هـ. قيد بالدعوى لأنهم إذا شهدوا بمنقول أنه ملك المدعي تقبل وإن لم يشهدوا أنه في يد المدعى عليه بغير حق، لأنهم لما شهدوا بالملك وملك الإنسان لا يكون في يد غيره إلا بعارض، والبينة تكون على مدعي العارض ولا تكون على صاحب الأصل. وقال بعضهم: ما لم يشهدوا أنه في يد المدعى عليه بغير حق لا تقطع يد المدعى عليه، والأول أصح. وفيما سوى العقار لا يشترط أن يشهدوا أنه في يد المدعى عليه لأن القاضي يراه في يده فلا حاجة إلى البيان؛ كذا في المحيط والخانية.

قوله: (ولا تثبت اليد في العقار بتصادقهما بل ببينة أو علم القاضي بخلاف المنقول) نفياً لتهمة المواضعة، إذ العقار عساه في يد غيرهما بخلاف المنقول لأن اليد فيه مشاهدة. قيد بالدعوى لما في شهادات البزازية: شهدوا أنه ملكه ولم يقولوا في يده بغير حق يفتى بالقبول. قال الصدر الأجل الحلواني: اختلف فيه المشايخ، والصحيح أنه لا يقبل لأنه إن لم يثبت يده بغير حق لا يمكنه المطالبة بالتسليم، وبه كان يفتي أكثر المشايخ. وقيل: يقضى في المنقول ولا يقضى في العقار حتى يقولوا إنه في يده بغير حق، والصحيح الذي عليه الفتوى أنه يقبل في حق القضاء بالملك لا في حق المطالبة بالتسليم، حتى قالوا: لو سأل القاضي الشاهد أهو في يد المدعى عليه بغير حق فقال لا أدري، يقبل على الملك؛ نص عليه في المحيط. وفي دعوى البزازية معزياً إلى الصغرى: ادعى على آخر ضيعة أنها له، فأقر المدعى عليه أنها في يده، وبرهن المدعي على أنها ملكه، فحكم الحاكم بالملك له، لا يصح ما لم يثبت اليد بالبينة أو يعلم الحاكم. وفيه قال: المدعى عليه ليس العقار في يدي، يحلفه حتى يقر، فإذا أقر باليد يحلفه أنها ليست ملكه حتى يقر بالملك للمدعي، فإذا أقر له به يأمره بترك التعرض، لكن إن أراد أن يبرهن أنها ملكه لا بد من تقديم البينة على أنها في يده لأن المالك قد يبعد عن العقار عادة، فأمكن أن يتواضع اثنان ويقر أحدهما باليد ويبرهن الآخر عليه بالملك ويسامح في الشهود ثم يدفع المالك معللاً بحكم الحاكم، وهذه التهمة في المنقول منتف لأن يد المالك لا تنقطع عن المنقول عادة بل يكون في يده. فاندفع به ما قيل في شرح الوقاية: تهمة المواضعة ثابتة في الموضعين على السواء فيقضى في المنقول بإقراره باليد كما صرح به جميع الكتب ا.هـ. وهكذا في الخانية. وبه علم أن ثبوت اليد بالبينة أو العلم في العقار إنما هو لصحة القضاء بالملك المدعى عليه في دعوى الملك دون دعوى الضمان، وكذا دون دعوى الشراء كما سينبه عليه. قوله: (فاندفع به ما قيل في شرح الوقاية) أجاب في الدرر عن اعتراض الوقاية واعترضه محشوه، وللمحقق سعدى جلبي في حواشي الهداية تحقيق نفيس في هذا المحل فراجعه.

يمنع [؟] أما بالبينة لا لصحة الدعوى كما هو ظاهر المتون، ولو كان لها لم يحلف قبله كما لا يخفى. ثم ذكر في الخامس عشر من أنواع الدعاوى: الدعوى في العقار إنما يحتاج إلى إثبات يد المدعى عليه في العقار إذا ادعاه بالملك المطلق، أما إذا ادعى الشراء منه وإقراره بأنه في يده فأنكر الشراء وأقر بكونه في يده لا يحتاج إلى إعادة البينة على كونه في يده، والفرق أن دعوى الفعل كما تصح على ذي اليد تصح على غيره أيضاً، فإنه يدعي علته التمليك والتملك، وهو كما يتحقق من ذي اليد يتحقق من غيره أيضاً، فعدم ثبوت اليد بالإقرار لا لصحة الدعوى. الملك المطلق فدعوى ترك التعرض بإزالة اليد وطلب إزالتها لا يتصور إلا من صاحب دعوى اليد، وبإقراره لا يثبت كونه ذا يد لاحتمال المواضعة كما قررناه من قبل ا.هـ. والحاصل أن اشتراط ثبوت اليد في العقار إنما هو في دعوى الملك المطلق، أما في دعوى الغصب والشراء فلا. وفي الخانية: فالحاصل أن دعوى الملك في العقار لا تسمع إلا على صاحب اليد، ودعوى اليد تقبل على غير صاحب اليد إذا كان ذلك الغير ينازعه في اليد فيجعل مدعياً لليد مقصوداً ومدعياً للملك تبعاً لملك اليد ا.هـ. وقد ظهر بما ذكرناه وأطلقه أصحاب المتون أنه يصح دعوى الملك المطلق في العقار بلا بيان سبب الملك. وفي دعوى البزازية من فصل التناقض: واعلم أن مشايخ فرغانة ذكروا أن الشرط في دعوى العقار في بلاد قدم بناؤها بيان السبب، ولا تسمع فيه دعوى الملك المطلق لوجوه: الأول أن دعوى الملك المطلق دعوى الخطة، ومعلوم أن صاحب الخطة في مثل تلك البلاد غير موجود، والأصل بسبب من فيكون كذباً لا محالة فكيف يقضى به؟ والثاني أنه لما تعذر القضاء بالمطلق لما قلنا فلا بد من أن يقضي بالملك بسبب، وذلك إما سبب مجهول أو معلوم، فالمجهول لا يمكن القضاء والمعلوم لعدم تعيين المدعي إياه. والثالث أن الاستحقاق لو فرض بسبب حادث يجوز أن يكون ذلك السبب شراء ذي اليد من آخر، ثم يجوز أن يكون السبب سابقاً على تملك ذي اليد فيمنع الرجوع، ويجوز أن يكون لاحقاً فلا يمنع الرجوع فيشتبه. وكل هذه الرواية غير متحقق في المنقول لعدم المانع من الحمل على التملك الأصل ا.هـ. قوله: (وأنه يطالبه) أي وذكر المدعي أنه يطالب المدعى عليه بالمدعي، لأن المطالبة حقه فلا بد من طلبه، ولأنه يحتمل أن يكون مرهوناً في يده أو محبوساً بالثمن في يده، وإنما يزول هذا الاحتمال بالمطالبة. قوله: (وإن كان ديناً ذكر وصفه) لأنه لا بد من تعريفه، وهو بالوصف. أطلقه فشمل. قوله: (والحاصل أن اشتراط الخ) وأقول: هذه المسألة تقع كثيراً ويغفل القضاة عنها في زماننا حيث لا يتعرضون إلى البينة على اليد مطلقاً، فلذا نظمتها بقولي:
واليد لا تثبت في العقار التصادق فلا تماري مع
فيلزم البرهان ما لم يدع عليه غصباً أو شراء
The judge contrary to movable property and that he demands him and if it is a debt he mentions its description and that he demands him with it. Then if it is valid, it is required that he clarify that the entire house is in the hand of the defendant? It is said it is required because usurpation of half as undivided share is only possible with the whole being in his hand. And it is said usurpation of half as undivided share is conceivable if the house is between them and one usurps from the other, so it is usurpation of half as undivided share. End. It is restricted to the claim because when they testify regarding movable property that it is the plaintiff's property, it is accepted even if they do not testify that it is in the defendant's hand without right, because when they testified to ownership, and a person's property is not in another's hand except due to some impediment, and the evidence is on the one who claims the impediment, not on the owner. And some said: As long as they do not testify that it is in the defendant's hand without right, the defendant's hand is not cut off, and the first is more correct. And in other than immovable property, it is not required that they testify that it is in the defendant's hand because the judge sees it in his hand, so there is no need for clarification; thus in al-Muhit and al-Khaniya. His statement: (Possession in immovable property is not established by their mutual agreement but by evidence or the judge's knowledge, unlike movable property) to negate the suspicion of collusion, because immovable property may be in the hand of someone else, unlike movable property because possession in it is observable. He restricted it to the claim because in the testimonies of al-Bazzaziyya: They testified that it is his property and did not say it is in his hand without right, a fatwa is given for acceptance. Sadr al-Ajal al-Halwani said: The scholars differed on this, and the correct view is that it is not accepted, because if his hand is not proven without right, he cannot claim delivery, and most scholars used to give fatwa accordingly. And it is said: Judgment is given for movable property but not for immovable property until they say it is in his hand without right, and the correct view on which fatwa is given is that it is accepted with regard to judgment of ownership, not with regard to claiming delivery. Hence they said: If the judge asks the witness 'Is it in the defendant's hand without right?' and he says 'I don't know', it is accepted for ownership; so stated in al-Muhit. And in the claim of al-Bazzaziyya attributed to al-Sughra: Someone claimed a land property from another that it is his, so the defendant admitted that it is in his hand, and the plaintiff proved that it is his property, so the judge ruled ownership for him. This is not valid unless possession is proven by evidence or the judge knows. And in it he said: The defendant says 'The property is not in my hand', he administers an oath until he admits. When he admits possession, he administers an oath that it is not his property until he admits ownership for the plaintiff. When he admits it for him, he orders him to cease interference. But if he wants to prove that it is his property, he must present evidence that it is in his hand because the owner is usually distant from immovable property. So it is possible for two to collude: one admits possession and the other proves ownership with permissive witnesses, then the owner is removed justified by the judge's ruling. This suspicion is absent in movable property because the owner's hand is usually not separated from movable property; rather it is in his hand. Thus what was said in Sharh al-Wiqaya is refuted: The suspicion of collusion is equally present in both cases, so judgment is given in movable property by his admission of possession as all books have clarified. End. And likewise in al-Khaniya. And from it, it is known that proving possession by evidence or knowledge in immovable property is only for the validity of judgment of the claimed ownership in a claim of ownership, not in a claim of guarantee, nor in a claim of purchase as will be indicated. His statement: (Thus what was said in Sharh al-Wiqaya is refuted) He answered in al-Durar the objection of al-Wiqaya and his annotator objected, and the researcher Sa'di Jlibi has a precious investigation in his glosses on al-Hidaya on this point, so consult it. It is prevented? As for by evidence, it is not for the validity of the claim as is apparent from the texts, and if it were, he would not swear him before it as is not hidden. Then he mentioned in the fifteenth of the types of claims: The claim regarding immovable property only requires proving the hand of the defendant in immovable property if he claims it by absolute ownership. But if he claims purchase from him and his admission that it is in his hand, and he denies the purchase and admits that it is in his hand, he does not need to repeat the evidence on it being in his hand. The difference is that a claim of an action is valid against the possessor and also against others; because he claims its cause, which is giving ownership and taking ownership, and this is realized from the possessor and also from others. So failure to prove possession by admission is not for the validity of the claim. Absolute ownership: the claim of ceasing interference by removing the hand and seeking its removal is only conceivable from the one claiming the hand, and by his admission he does not prove being the possessor due to possibility of collusion as we established before. End. The conclusion is that requiring proof of possession in immovable property is only in claims of absolute ownership, but in claims of usurpation and purchase, it is not required. And in al-Khaniya: The conclusion is that a claim of ownership in immovable property is only heard against the possessor, and a claim of possession is accepted against a non-possessor if that other disputes possession, so he is considered as claiming possession as the objective and claiming ownership as subsidiary to possession of the hand. End. And it has become clear from what we mentioned and the authors of the texts have stated absolutely that a claim of absolute ownership in immovable property is valid without specifying the cause of ownership. And in al-Bazzaziyya from the chapter of contradiction: Know that the scholars of Farghana mentioned that the condition in a claim of immovable property in cities whose buildings are old is to specify the cause, and a claim of absolute ownership is not heard there for several reasons: First, a claim of absolute ownership is a claim of planning (khatta), and it is known that the owner of planning in such cities no longer exists, and the origin is by some cause, so it would be undoubtedly false, so how can judgment be given on it? Second, since judgment on absolute ownership is impossible for what we said, it must be judged by ownership with a cause, and that cause is either unknown or known; the unknown cannot be adjudicated, and the known because the plaintiff did not specify it. Third, if entitlement is supposed by a later cause, it is possible that the cause is purchase from the possessor from another, then it is possible that the cause precedes the possession of the possessor and thus prevents recourse, and it is possible that it is subsequent and does not prevent recourse, so it becomes ambiguous. All this problem is not realized in movable property because there is no impediment to attributing it to original ownership. End. His statement: (And that he demands him) i.e., the plaintiff mentions that he demands the defendant with the claimed property, because the demand is his right, so his request is necessary, and because it is possible that it is mortgaged in his hand or detained for the price in his hand, and this possibility is only removed by the demand. His statement: (And if it is a debt, he mentions its description) because it must be defined, and it is by description. He made it absolute so it includes. His statement: (And the conclusion that the condition...) And I say: This issue occurs frequently and judges in our time are neglectful of it, as they do not address evidence on possession at all, so I versified it in my saying: Possession is not established in immovable property by mutual agreement, so do not argue, so evidence is necessary unless he claims against him usurpation or purchase.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed commentary on a line from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the establishment of possession (yad) in court claims. The central ruling is that possession in immovable property (aqar) cannot be proven by mutual agreement (tasadiq) of the parties; rather, it requires evidence (bayyina) or the judge's personal knowledge (ilm al-qadi). This is to prevent collusion (tuhmat al-muwada'a), where two parties might falsely agree on possession to fraudulently transfer ownership. In contrast, possession of movable property (manqul) is established by mutual agreement because the judge can physically observe the property in the defendant's hand. The passage then addresses a dispute among Hanafi scholars: Is it necessary for witnesses to explicitly state that the property is in the defendant's hand 'without right' (bi-ghayr haqq)? Some scholars, including a majority, require this for the claim of delivery (taslim), but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that testimony is accepted for proving ownership (malikiyya) even without this phrase, though it is required for enforcing delivery. The commentary also distinguishes between different types of claims. In a claim of absolute ownership (da'wa al-milk al-mutlaq), proving possession is essential because the plaintiff seeks to remove the defendant's hand. But in claims based on a specific cause (sabab), such as usurpation (ghasb) or purchase (shira'), possession need not be proven because the claim is about an action that can be directed against anyone, not just the possessor. Finally, the passage mentions a regional opinion from scholars of Farghana that in old cities, absolute ownership claims are invalid because original ownership from planning (khatta) no longer exists; thus, the plaintiff must specify the cause of ownership. This view is not adopted by the majority.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control over property; in legal context, it refers to the fact that the property is in someone's physical or legal control.
عقار (aqar): Immovable property such as land and buildings.
منقول (manqul): Movable property that can be transported.
مواضعة (muwada'a): Collusion or pretense between two parties to deceive the court, especially in property claims.
دعوى الملك المطلق (da'wa al-milk al-mutlaq): A claim of absolute ownership without specifying how the property was acquired.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is possession in immovable property not established by mutual agreement of the parties?
A) Because the judge cannot see immovable property.
B) To prevent collusion between the parties.
C) Because immovable property cannot be owned without evidence.
D) Because the defendant must always have the property in hand.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, possession in immovable property must be proven by evidence or judge's knowledge to counteract potential collusion, a requirement that applies specifically to claims of absolute ownership.

Condition of Possession in Real Estate Claims and the Farghana Dispute
Expansion on possession condition in real estate claims and intra-Hanafi disagreement on stating the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish absolute ownership claims from claims based on a specific cause, explain why proof of possession is required only for the former, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on requiring the cause in old cities.

Core Idea
Real estate claims require proof of defendant's possession only for absolute ownership, not for usurpation or purchase.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical distinction in real estate litigation: the claimant's basis for ownership determines whether the claimant must first prove the defendant's possession. The text then introduces an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether in old cities, a claimant must specify the cause of ownership. Understanding this distinction and the limits of the dispute is essential for accurate fatwa and judicial practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of claims
The text distinguishes between a claim of absolute ownership (مُلْك مُطْلَق) without specifying how ownership arose, and a claim based on a specific action like usurpation (غَصْب) or purchase (شِرَاء). Recognize that the ruling differs for each.
Absolute ownership claims are about the property itself; action-based claims are about a specific event.
2
Understand the rationale for the difference
A claim of action (e.g., 'you bought from me') can be true against any defendant, possessor or not. But a claim of absolute ownership inherently relates to possession: you can only demand removal of a hand if the hand is there. Hence, proof of possession is needed for absolute ownership claims.
The text says: 'دعوى الفعل كما تصح على ذي اليد تصح على غيره' – a claim of action is valid against both possessor and non-possessor.
3
State the general Hanafi rule on proving possession
For a claim of absolute ownership in real estate, the claimant must prove that the defendant is in possession (يَد). For claims of usurpation or purchase, no such proof is required; the claim can proceed directly.
This is the position of the 'اصحاب المتون' (authors of the standard texts) and is considered the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.
4
Analyze the Farghana dispute
The scholars of Farghana (within the Hanafi school) require the claimant to state the cause (سَبَب) of ownership in old cities (بلاد قدم بناؤها). They give three arguments: 1) absolute ownership claims in old cities are inevitably false because original owners don't exist; 2) without a cause, the judge cannot specify the basis of judgment; 3) the cause could be later or earlier, creating confusion. Understand that this is a regional variation.
This is not the general Hanafi view. The author sides with the general view but does not dismiss the Farghana opinion as invalid.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The author ('ashab al-mutun') and the text indicate that the general position (no need to state cause) is stronger, citing the possibility of original ownership (تملك الأصل) and lack of textual obstacle. However, the dispute remains legitimate within the school, and in practice, some judges may follow the Farghana view.
The author ends with a poetic reminder that judges today neglect the requirement of proving possession, which is the real issue.

Book of Claims. He prevents? Indeed by evidence not for validity of claim as is apparent from the texts, and if it were for her he would not administer an oath before it, as is not hidden. Then he mentioned in the fifteenth of the types of claims: claim regarding real estate: It only requires establishing the possession of the defendant in real estate when he claims absolute ownership. But if he claims purchase from him and his acknowledgment that it is in his hand, and he denies the purchase but acknowledges it is in his hand, he does not need to bring evidence again on its being in his hand. The difference is that a claim of an action, just as it is valid against the possessor, is also valid against others, because he claims the cause of ownership and being owned, and just as it is realized from the possessor, it is realized from others as well. So the non-establishment of possession by acknowledgment does not invalidate the claim of absolute ownership. Thus the claim of refraining from interference by removing the hand and demanding its removal can only be conceived from the claimant of possession, and by his acknowledgment his being a possessor is not established due to the possibility of collusion as we established before. Second, since judgment based on absolute ownership is impossible for what we said, it must be judged by ownership due to a cause, and that cause is either unknown or known. The unknown cannot be judged, and the known is not specified by the claimant. Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the conditions for valid claims in real estate (aqar). The author begins by clarifying that the requirement of proving the defendant's possession (yad) applies specifically to claims of absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq) without specifying a cause. If the claimant bases his claim on a specific action like usurpation (ghasb) or purchase (shira'), he need not first prove the defendant's possession because the action itself can be attributed to anyone, not just the current possessor. The quote from Al-Khaniyya reinforces this: a claim of ownership is only heard against the possessor, but a claim of possession can be brought against a non-possessor if there is a dispute over possession. The text then presents a disagreement between the general Hanafi position (as stated by ashab al-mutun) and the scholars of Farghana, who in old cities require the claimant to state a specific cause (sabab) for his claim of ownership, because absolute ownership claims in such contexts are inevitably false due to the impossibility of tracing ownership back to original settlement. The author also includes a brief note on the requirement of demand (matalaba), explaining that the claimant must explicitly ask for the defendant to be ordered to pay, as this removes possibilities like the property being held as collateral. He ends with a poetic summary emphasizing that possession in real estate is not established by mere mutual acknowledgment; proof is required unless the claim is based on usurpation or purchase.

Key Terms
الدعوى (al-da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit brought before a judge.
اليد (al-yad): Possession or control over property; a legal basis for claims.
الملك المطلق (al-mulk al-mutlaq): Absolute ownership without specifying the cause of acquisition.
السبب (al-sabab): The cause or legal basis for ownership, such as purchase, inheritance, gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Proof of the defendant's possession

D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
For real estate, absolute ownership claims require proving the defendant's possession; claims of usurpation or purchase do not. The Farghana addition of requiring a cause is a legitimate regional dispute, but the general position is mufta bihi.

The Requirements for a Valid Debt Claim in Hanafi Law
Lists conditions for valid debt claim and addresses dispute on demand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five required elements for a valid debt claim of fungible items, explain why the cause (sabab) is necessary, and evaluate the dispute regarding the demand (matalaba).

Core Idea
A valid debt claim must specify genus, type, quality, quantity, and cause; demand is not essential according to the mufta bihi.

When you claim a debt in court, not all statements are accepted. The Hanafi school requires precise details to ensure the claim is actionable. In this lesson, we will unpack the elements that make a debt claim valid, the logic behind requiring the cause, and a key internal dispute about whether you must explicitly demand payment. Understanding these conditions will help you see how Hanafi procedure protects both claimants and defendants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five required descriptions for mithliyyat
From the passage, extract the five elements that must be mentioned in a claim for fungible items: genus, type, quality, quantity, and cause of obligation. Note the authority cited (Al-Khazana).
Mithliyyat are items like wheat or money that have standard equivalents.
2
Explain why the cause (sabab) is necessary
The text gives different places of fulfillment depending on the cause: salam, qard, sale price, ghasb, etc. Understand that the cause determines where the debt must be paid, so it must be stated.
E.g., for a loan, place of contract is where payment is due.
3
Analyze the special conditions for a loan claim
In a loan claim, the claimant must mention that the lender lent from his own property and that the borrower applied it to his needs (per Abu Yusuf). This ensures the debt is binding.
Abu Yusuf's view: loan becomes debt only when spent; majority (Abu Hanifa) holds contract alone creates debt.
4
Evaluate the dispute over the demand (matalaba)
While early texts require the claimant to say 'give me my right', later fatwa works (Al-Khulasa, Al-Bazzaziyya) consider this condition weak. Abu Nasr holds the claim is valid without it. Identify the mufta bihi.
The author notes deficiency in earlier texts for not covering all conditions.
5
Recognize additional conditions for special claims
The passage mentions claims for goods, deposit, mudaraba, partnership, and guarantee, each with specific required details (e.g., value at death, cash vs. goods, cause for kafala). Acknowledge these without going into full detail.
These are extensions of the general rule; the reason is the same: specificity prevents ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والبزازية فجعلوا اشتراطه قولاً ضعيفاً. قال في الخلاصة : رجل ادعى على آخر عشرة دراهم عند القاضي وقال لي عليه عشرة دراهم ولم يزد على هذا اختلف المشايخ فيه قال بعضهم: الدعوى صحيحة. وقال بعضهم لا يصح ما لم يقل مرة ليعطني حقي هذا في النوازل قال أبو نصر : الصحيح أنه تسمع الدعوى .اهـ. ومثله في البزازية ولم أر أحداً نبه عليه . ثم اعلم في كلام أصحاب المتون والشروح في الدعوى قصوراً فإنهم لم يبينوا بقية شرائط دعوى الدين ولم يذكروا دعوى العقد؛ أما الأول ففي دعوى البضاعة والوديعة بسبب الموت مجهلاً لا بد أن يبين قيمته يوم موته إذ الواجب عليه قيمته يوم موته، وفي دعوى مال المضاربة بموت المضارب مجهلاً لا مضمون بالمثل والمشتري بمال الشركة مضمون بالقيمة، ولو ادعى مالاً بكفالة لا بد من بيان المال أنه بأي سبب لجواز بطلانها إذ الكفالة بنفقة المرأة إذا لم تذكر مدة معلومة لا تصح إلا أن يقول ما عشت أو ما دمت في نكاحه والكفالة بمال الكتابة لا تصح وكذا بالدية على العاقلة ولا بد أن يقول وأجاز المكفول له الكفالة، قوله : (وفي دعوى السعاية أي السعاية به إلى الحاكم.
Measured and weighed items, cash and otherwise. We have already mentioned that in a claim of fungible items (mithliyyat), one must mention the genus, type, quality, quantity, and cause of obligation. Hence it is said in Al-Khazana: If someone claims ten measures of wheat as a debt upon him and does not mention by what cause, it is not heard, and it is necessary to mention the cause. Because if it is due to salam (forward sale), then he has the right to demand only in the place we specified; if due to qard (loan) or because it is the price of a sale, the place of the loan or sale is the place of fulfillment; if due to usurpation or consumption, he has the right to demand delivery of the wheat in the place of usurpation or consumption. And in it: In a claim of loan, he mentions that the lender lent him such-and-such from his own property, and it should be permissible that he was an agent for lending, and the agent for lending is a messenger and a lender (mu'ir) and cannot demand payment. He also mentions that the borrower used it for his own needs so that it becomes a debt upon him by consensus, because according to Abu Yusuf, the borrower does not become liable for a debt in his dhimmah except by spending it on his own needs. In a loan, mentioning the place of fulfillment is not required, and the place of the contract is determined. As for a claim due to acknowledgment (iqrar) regarding a specific thing (ayn) or debt, the mufta bihi according to the mashayikh is that if it is on the side of entitlement (istihqaq), it is not heard; if on the side of defense (daf'), it is heard. The full details are in Al-Bazzaziyya and Al-Khazana. His statement: (And that he demands him for it) for what we said, and because the person with liability is present, so nothing remains except the demand. This is firmly stated in the texts and commentaries. But the intended meaning is not the specific wording 'I demand him' but rather any phrasing that indicates 'give me my right'. As for the authors of fatawa, as in Al-Khulasa and Al-Bazzaziyya, they consider this condition weak. He said in Al-Khulasa: A man claimed against another ten dirhams before the judge and said 'he owes me ten dirhams' and did not add more; the mashayikh differed about this. Some said the claim is valid; some said it is not valid unless he says once 'give me my right'. This is in Al-Nawazil. Abu Nasr said: The correct view is that the claim is heard. And similar is in Al-Bazzaziyya, and I have not seen anyone drawing attention to it. Then know that the speech of the authors of the texts and commentaries regarding the claim is deficient: they did not explain the remaining conditions of a debt claim and did not mention claims based on contract. As for the first: In a claim of goods (bidha'a) or deposit (wadi'a) due to death without specification, he must state its value on the day of death, because the obligation is its value on that day. In a claim of mudaraba capital upon the death of the mudarib without specification, he must mention whether the capital on the day of death was cash or goods, because if it was goods, he has the authority to claim the value. In a claim of partnership capital upon the death of a partner without specification, he must mention whether the deceased died with partnership property or with property purchased with partnership funds, because partnership property is guaranteed by the like, and property purchased with partnership funds is guaranteed by value. And if he claims property due to guarantee (kafala), he must mention the property by what cause, because it may be invalid: for instance, guarantee of a woman's maintenance without a specified term is not valid unless he says 'as long as she lives' or 'as long as she is in his marriage'; guarantee of kitaba property is not valid; similarly for blood money on the 'aqila; and he must say that the guaranteed by the guarantee. His statement: (And in the claim of sa'aya) i.e., the exertion to bring it to the judge.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the requirements for a valid debt claim (da'wa bi dayn). The author begins by confirming that for fungible items (mithliyyat like wheat), the claimant must specify the genus (jins), type (naw'), quality (sifah), quantity (qadr), and the cause of obligation (sabab wujub). The cause is critical because it determines the place of fulfillment: for salam (forward sale), it is the specified place; for qard (loan) or price of sale, it is the place of contract; for ghasb (usurpation) or istihlak (consumption), it is the place where the property was taken. In a loan claim, the claimant must also mention that the lender lent from his own property and that the borrower used it for his own needs, since according to Imam Abu Yusuf, a loan only becomes a binding debt when the borrower spends it. For claims based on acknowledgment (iqrar), there is a distinction: if the acknowledgment is on the side of entitlement (i.e., the claimant is asserting a right), it is not heard; if it is on the side of defense (i.e., the defendant acknowledges a debt to ward off a claim), it is heard. The author then discusses the condition of demand (matalaba). While the texts and commentaries firmly require the claimant to demand payment (e.g., saying 'give me my right'), many fatwa collections (like Al-Khulasa) consider this condition weak, and the correct view is that the claim is valid without explicit demand as long as it is clear that the claimant is asserting a right. The author then notes that the texts are deficient in not covering all conditions of debt claims, such as those involving bidha'a (goods), wadi'a (deposit), mudaraba, shirka (partnership), and kafala (guarantee). For each, specific details must be mentioned: for goods or deposit upon death, the value at death; for mudaraba, whether the capital was cash or goods; for partnership, whether the property was partnership property or purchased with partnership funds; for guarantee, the specific cause to avoid invalidity. The passage ends with a note on sa'aya (claim of exertion to bring the matter to court).

Key Terms
المثليات (al-mithliyyat): Fungible items that can be replaced by similar items, like wheat or money.
سبب الوجوب (sabab al-wujub): The cause or legal reason for the debt, such as loan, sale, or tort.
المطالبة (al-matalaba): The demand for payment by the claimant.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the passage, is a debt claim valid without the claimant explicitly saying 'give me my right'?
A) No, because the texts require it.
B) Yes, as long as the claimant asserts his right.
C) Yes, but only for fungible items.
D) No, except for loans.

Key Takeaway
A valid Hanafi debt claim must specify genus, type, quality, quantity, and cause; the cause is crucial for determining the place of fulfillment, and explicit demand is not required according to the mufta bihi.

Qada — Judiciary

Judge's Duty After a Valid Claim: Questioning, Iqrar vs Bayyinah, and Adab
Explains judge's duty and qada' roles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the judge's obligation to question the defendant after a valid claim and distinguish between the role of qada' in cases of iqrar and bayyinah.

Core Idea
A valid claim obliges the judge to question the defendant; qada' is only needed for bayyinah, not iqrar.

This lesson explains the judge's procedural duties after a valid claim is presented. According to the Hanafi school, the judge must ask the defendant to respond, even if the claimant does not explicitly request it. The passage also clarifies a critical usul point: a judicial ruling (qada') is only required to make testimony conclusive, whereas a confession (iqrar) is self-binding. Finally, certain formalities like the claimant requesting judgment or the judge pronouncing 'I rule' are mere etiquettes (adab), not legal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The judge must ask the defendant about the claim after a valid claim is established. The hukm level is wajib.
If the claim is invalid, no questioning is required.
2
Determine the basis for obligation
A valid claim obliges a response; an invalid claim does not. The judge's question is to uncover the truth.
The text implies that a valid claim creates a right to an answer.
3
Distinguish iqrar from bayyinah
In iqrar, the confession is itself conclusive proof and does not need a judicial ruling to be binding. In bayyinah, testimony is probable and only becomes conclusive through the judge's ruling.
This is why the word 'qada' is used metaphorically for iqrar.
4
Identify the adab elements
The claimant's explicit request for judgment and the judge's pronouncement 'I rule' are recommended etiquette (adab), not obligatory.
This is supported by al-Bazzaziyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإذا صحت الدعوى سأل المدعى عليه عنها لينكشف وجه الحكم ومفهومه أنها إذا لم تصح لا يسأله القاضي عنها لعدم وجوب الجواب عليها لها بخلاف الصحيحة فإنه يجب عليه جوابها، وظاهره أن القاضي يسأله وإن لم يطلب المدعي. وفي السراجية : إذا حضر الخصمان لا بأس أن يقول ما لكما وإن شاء سكت حتى يبتدأه بالكلام وإذا تكلم المدعي يسكت الآخر مقالته فإذا فرغ يقول للمدعى عليه بطلب المدعي ماذا تقول، وقيل إن ويسمع المدعي إذا كان جاهلاً فإن القاضي يسأل المدعى عليه بدون طلب المدعي اهـ. وفي شهادات الخزانة : يجوز للقاضي أن يأمر رجلاً يعلم المدعي الدعوى والخصومة إذا كان لا يقدر عليها ولا يحسنها اهـ . وفي القنية : ليس للقاضي أن يمنع ذا اليد عن التصرف في الضيعة بالدعوى وطلب المدعي ذلك اهـ. وسيأتي قوله : (فإن أقر أو أنكر فبرهن المدعي قضى عليه) لوجود الحجة الملزمة للقضاء. وفي المعراج ولفظ القضاء في الإقرار مجاز للزومه بإقراره فلا حاجة إلى القضاء لكونه حجة بنفسه لا يتوقف على القضاء فكان الحكم إلزاماً للخروج عن موجبه بخلاف البينة فإن الشهادة خبر محتمل وبالقضاء يصير حجة ويسقط احتمال الكذب اهـ. ولم پشترط المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى طلب الخصم القضاء بعد الحجة لما في البزازية : ويعلم المدعى عليه أنه يريد القضاء وهذا أدب غير لازم وكذا قول القاضي أحكم أدب غير لازم اهـ.
His statement: (When the claim is valid, he asks the defendant about it) to uncover the aspect of the ruling. Its implication is that if it is not valid, the judge does not ask him about it because no answer is required for it, unlike the valid claim, for which an answer is obligatory. The apparent meaning is that the judge asks him even if the claimant does not request it. In al-Sirajiyyah: When the two litigants are present, there is no harm in the judge saying, 'What is the matter with you both?' or if he wishes, he may remain silent until one begins speaking. When the claimant speaks, the other is silent until he finishes. Then, at the request of the claimant, he says to the defendant, 'What do you say?' It is also said that he listens to the claimant if he is ignorant, and the judge asks the defendant without the claimant's request. End quote. In Shihadat al-Khazanah: It is permissible for the judge to order a man to teach the claimant the claim and litigation if he is unable to do so and does not know how. End quote. In al-Qunyah: The judge may not prevent the holder of possession from disposing of the estate due to the claim and the claimant's request. End quote. His statement will come: (If he admits or denies and the claimant provides proof, judgment is given against him) because there is evidence that compels judgment. In al-Mi'raj: The word 'judgment' (qada') in the case of iqrar is metaphorical, because [the liability] is binding by his iqrar, so there is no need for judgment, since iqrar is proof by itself and does not depend on judgment. Thus, the ruling is an obligation to exit from its requirement, unlike the bayyinah, for testimony is a probable report, and by judgment it becomes proof and the possibility of falsehood is eliminated. End quote. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not require the opponent to request judgment after the evidence, as in al-Bazzaziyyah: He informs the defendant that he wants judgment, and this is non-obligatory etiquette (adab). Similarly, the judge's saying 'I rule' is non-obligatory etiquette. End quote.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the judge's procedure after a valid claim. The author explains that the judge must ask the defendant about the claim because only a valid claim obliges an answer. Interestingly, the judge should do this even if the claimant does not explicitly request it, though there is an opinion that if the claimant is ignorant, the judge may actively help. The author cites al-Sirajiyyah for the etiquettes of court proceedings, allowing the judge to initiate or remain silent. He also permits the judge to assist an ignorant claimant by arranging for someone to teach him. The passage then discusses the nature of judgment: for iqrar (confession), the defendant's admission is self-sufficient and does not require a judicial ruling to be binding; the judge merely enforces it. For bayyinah (evidence), however, the judge's ruling is necessary to transition testimony into conclusive proof. Finally, the author notes that requiring the claimant to say 'I want judgment' or the judge to say 'I rule' is mere adab (etiquette) and not obligatory, as stated in al-Bazzaziyyah.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Judicial ruling or judgment, which transforms testimony into conclusive proof but is not needed for iqrar.
أدب (adab): Non-obligatory recommended behaviour or etiquette in legal proceedings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the status of a judge saying 'I rule' after evidence is presented?
A) It is obligatory (wajib)
B) It is non-obligatory etiquette (adab)
C) It is prohibited (haram)
D) It is invalid (batil)

Key Takeaway
After a valid claim, the judge must question the defendant; but confessing (iqrar) does not require a judicial ruling to be binding, whereas testimony (bayyinah) does.

The Basic Lawsuit Procedure: Judge's Questioning and Defendant's Response
Core ruling on lawsuit procedure and judge's duty to question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the judge's duty to question the defendant upon a valid claim and distinguish the three possible responses (admission, denial with proof, oath).

Core Idea
A valid lawsuit obligates the judge to ask the defendant; admission or proof leads to judgment, otherwise an oath is administered.

This lesson unpacks the opening rule of the chapter on lawsuits (da'wa). The Hanafi jurists established a clear procedure: when a valid claim is brought, the judge must question the defendant. The defendant either admits, denies and the plaintiff provides proof, or an oath is administered. The commentaries expand on the judge's duty even without the plaintiff's request and provide for assisting an ignorant plaintiff.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
The text begins with the basic rule: the judge asks the defendant about the claim. Determine the legal nature of this action—is it obligatory or discretionary? The commentary indicates it is obligatory for a valid claim.
The word 'سأل' is imperative in form, but the context confirms obligation.
2
List the defendant's possible responses
The defendant may admit (iqrar), deny (inkar) followed by plaintiff's proof (bayyinah), or refuse both, leading to an oath (yamin). Note that admission is self-sufficient; no judgment is required.
Admission does not need a judge's ruling; it is binding by itself.
3
Recognize the judge's proactive role
The commentary clarifies that the judge must ask the defendant even if the plaintiff does not explicitly request it. This is derived from the need to uncover the truth.
This is a key procedural safeguard in Hanafi law.
4
Examine exceptions and assistance
The judge may help an ignorant plaintiff by having someone teach him how to present the case. Also, the judge cannot prevent the possessor from disposing of property based solely on a claim.
These exceptions maintain balance between justice and property rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The book of lawsuit: The lawsuit: he asks the defendant about it. If he admits or denies and the plaintiff brings proof, he judges against him; otherwise he administers an oath. The session of suretyship, such that if he said in his session it is not permissible. And if a woman claims property against the heirs of the husband, it is not valid unless she explains the reason, because it is possible it is a debt of maintenance, which is extinguished by his death. And in a claim of debt against a deceased, if it is recorded that he died without paying it and leaves from the estate in the hand of this heir what is sufficient, this claim is heard even if he does not specify the specific assets of the estate; and according to this fatwa is given, but the judge only orders the heir to pay the debt if it is established that the estate reached him. And if he denies that it reached him, it cannot be established except after specifying the specific assets of the estate in his hand in a way that provides information. And if he claims a debt due to inheritance, it is necessary to specify all his heirs. And in a claim of su'i (wages) it is not necessary to mention the receiver of the money, but in the document of her claim it is necessary to explain the su'i so that we see whether he is liable for guarantee, because it is possible he performed work for a right so he is not liable. And in mentioning mutual separation (takharij) and settlement (sulh) from the estate, it is necessary to specify the types of estate and renew the real estate and specify the value of each type so that it is known that the settlement did not exceed the value of his share, because if they consumed the estate and then settled with the claimant for more than his share, it is not permissible according to them, as in usurpation. End. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not require the opponent to request judgment after evidence, as in al-Bazzaziyya: The defendant is informed that he wants judgment, and this is etiquettes, not obligatory. Similarly, the judge's saying 'I judge' is etiquettes, not obligatory. End. And what is apparent from the book is that the judge does not grant a delay to the defendant if he requests delay, and it is nothing. In al-Bazzaziyya: He grants three days if the defendant says 'I have a defense.' He only grants this period because they used to sit every three days or a week. If he sits every day, yet he grants him three days, it is permissible. Then if the period passes and he does not provide the defense, he judges. End. Therefore we wrote in al-Fawa'id: It is not permissible for the judge to delay judgment after its conditions exist except in three [cases].
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is from the book of lawsuit (da'wa). It opens with the basic rule: when a valid claim is made, the judge asks the defendant. If the defendant admits or denies and the plaintiff brings evidence, the judge rules against him. Otherwise, the judge administers an oath. The commentary then elaborates on various conditions for hearing a claim, including the need for the claimant to specify the cause in certain situations such as a wife claiming against her husband's heirs. It discusses the necessity of detailing the estate when claiming a debt against a deceased, and other specific scenarios. The passage also explains that the judge should ask the defendant even if the plaintiff does not request, and that the judge may sometimes assist an ignorant plaintiff. It addresses the issue of granting the defendant time to prepare a defense, stating that the judge may grant three days if the defendant claims to have a defense. The fatwa is that delaying judgment is not permissible except in three cases.

Key Terms
الدعوى (al-da'wa): A claim or lawsuit brought by a plaintiff against a defendant before a judge.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'a alayh): The defendant, the person against whom a claim is made.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission or confession of the claim by the defendant.
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The plaintiff, the person who brings the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only if the plaintiff explicitly requests it.

C) Only if the claim is valid, even without the plaintiff's request.
D) Only if the defendant is present in court.

Key Takeaway
The judge must proactively question the defendant upon a valid lawsuit, ensuring the truth emerges regardless of the plaintiff's initiative.
